When Halal and Haram Combine: The Rule of Prohibition Prevailing
Introducing the rule of prohibition prevailing over permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال' to determine the ruling when permissible and prohibited elements are mixed in a single matter.

Core Idea
When halal and haram mix, prohibition wins — the entire thing is haram.

This passage introduces the second major maxim of the second type in Ibn Nujaym's Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. The rule states that when halal and haram combine, the haram prevails over the halal. Numerous applications from various fiqh chapters demonstrate its breadth, along with ten specific exceptions. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti to give correct fatwas in cases of mixture and uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the Arabic text of the rule: إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال. Recognize that it applies across all fiqh chapters, especially in food, marriage, worship, and commerce.
2
Identify the furuat (applications)
Note the examples given: a trained dog joined by an untrained one, a Magian assisting in slaughter, mixing of fat from a dead animal with oil, etc. Observe the pattern: in each case, the presence of a haram element makes the whole prohibited.
The examples are from chapters: sayd, dhaba'ih, taharah, at'imah, etc.
3
Analyze the underlying reasoning (illa)
The illa is that haram carries a stronger legal weight than halal. When both coexist, the prohibition dominates because of the principle of legal caution (ihtiyat) and the maxim 'al-mani' yuqaddam 'ala al-muqtadi' (the preventer is given precedence over the requirer).
This is related to the sub-rule mentioned at the end of the passage.
4
Recognize the istithna' (exceptions)
The text mentions that ten specific issues escape this rule. Though not enumerated here, these exceptions are detailed in the original work. A mufti must be aware that the rule is not absolute.
The exceptions are known as al-masa'il al-'ashr (the ten issues).
5
Apply the rule to a novel case
Given a new scenario where halal and haram are mixed, determine the ruling by asking: Is there a single compound matter? If yes, the haram element prevails, making the whole haram. If the scenario is one of the ten exceptions, the rule does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية : إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال وبيان ما تفرع عليها ممَّن اشتبه محرمه بأجنبيات، وما إذ كان أحد أبويه مأكولاً والآخر غير مأكول، وما إذا شارك الكلب المعلم غيره أو كلب المسلم كلب المجوسي، وما إذا وضع المجوسي يده على يد المسلم الذابح، وما إذا عجز المسلم عن مد قوسه فأعانه مجوسي، ووطء الجارية المشركة وما إذا كان بعض الشجرة أو الصيد في الحلّ وبعضها في الحرم وما إذا اختلطت المذكاة بالميتة وما إذا اختلط ودك الميتة بالزيت، وما إذا اختلطت زوجته بغيرها وفيه بيان ما إذا أسلم وتحته خمس، وما إذا صيدا فوقع في ماء أو سطح ثم على الأرض، وبيان ما خرج عنها من المسائل العشر، وفي آخرها تتمة فيما إذا جمع بين حلال وحرام في عقد أو نية، وبيان دخوله في أبواب النكاح والمهر والبيع والإجارة والكفالة والإبراء والهبة والهدية والوصية والإقرار والشهادة والقضاء والعبادات والطلاق والعتاق وعارية الرهن والوقف. وفي آخرها تنبيه على ما إذا اجتمع في العبادة جانب الحضر والسفر، ثم فصل في قاعدة إذا تعارض المانع والمقتضي فإنه يقدم المانع على المقتضي إلا في مسائل.
The second rule: When halal and haram combine, the haram prevails over the halal. And an explanation of what branches from it: regarding one whose prohibited women are confused with foreign women; and regarding an animal whose parents are one edible and the other non-edible; and regarding a trained dog that is joined by another dog, or a Muslim's dog joined by a Magian's dog; and regarding a Magian placing his hand on the hand of the Muslim slaughterer; and regarding a Muslim unable to draw his bow so a Magian assists him; and intercourse with a polytheist slave girl; and regarding when part of a tree or game is in the halal zone and part in the sanctuary; and when a properly slaughtered animal is mixed with a dead one; and when melted fat of a dead animal is mixed with oil; and when his wife is mixed with other women; and in it is an explanation of when a man converts to Islam while having five wives; and when game falls into water or onto a roof and then to the ground; and an explanation of what is excluded from it in ten issues, and at its end a supplement regarding when one combines halal and haram in a contract or intention, and an explanation of its entry into the chapters of marriage, dower, sale, hire, guarantee, discharge, gift, donation, bequest, confession, testimony, judgment, acts of worship, divorce, emancipation, loan of use, pledge, and endowment. And at its end is a notification regarding when in worship the aspects of residence and travel combine, and then a detailed discussion on the rule: when a preventer and a requirer conflict, the preventer is given precedence over the requirer except in some issues.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage introduces the second rule of the second type: 'إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال' (When halal and haram combine, the haram prevails over the halal). It means that if a situation has both permissible and prohibited elements, the prohibition takes precedence, and the whole is considered prohibited. Numerous applications are listed: confusion of prohibited women (e.g., sisters) with permissible ones; an animal with one edible parent and one non-edible; a trained hunting dog joined by another dog; a Magian assisting in slaughter; a Magian helping draw a bow; intercourse with a polytheist slave girl; game partly in the sanctuary; mixing of properly slaughtered meat with carrion; mixing of fat from a dead animal with oil; a wife confused with other women; a convert with five wives; game falling into water then to ground. The text mentions ten exceptions (مسائل عشر) that escape this rule. It also covers combinations of halal and haram in contracts and intentions across many fiqh chapters. Finally, it discusses the sub-rule about when a preventer (مانع) and a requirer (مقتضي) conflict, the preventer is given precedence except in certain cases.

Key Terms
الحلال (al-ḥalāl): That which is permissible according to Sharia.
الحرام (al-ḥarām): That which is prohibited; its presence renders the whole forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this rule, what happens when halal and haram combine?
A) The halal prevails
B) The haram prevails
C) Both are considered separately
D) It requires a new ijtihad

Key Takeaway
When halal and haram combine in a single compound, the haram element dominates, rendering the entire matter prohibited.

The Rule of Īthār in Acts of Devotion: Is It Disliked?
Introducing a qawida about īthār in devotional acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on īthār in devotional acts and identify when it is makruh and when it is permissible.

Core Idea
Giving preference to others in worship can be makruh if it means abandoning a personal obligation or virtue.

This lesson introduces the third rule of the second type of qawāʿid: whether īthār (giving preference to others over oneself) in acts of devotion (qurb) is disliked (makruh). The passage, though brief, points to a nuanced Hanafi position: īthār in worldly matters is praiseworthy, but in devotional acts it may be makruh if it involves neglecting one's own obligation or a stronger supererogatory act. Let us explore the rule, its scope, and its applications.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the rule: 'هل يُكره الإيثار بالقرب' (Is īthār in qurb disliked?). Understand that this is a question that requires a conditional answer—it is not always disliked.
The rule governs acts of devotion that bring one closer to Allah, such as prayer, fasting, charity, etc.
2
Differentiate between worldly and devotional īthār
In worldly matters (e.g., giving food to a guest though you need it), īthār is commendable. In devotional matters, the ruling depends on whether one abandons a personal obligation or a superior act.
The key distinction is the nature of the act: dutiful vs. supererogatory.
3
Identify conditions that make īthār disliked
Īthār in qurb is makruh if it leads to leaving a fard (obligation), a sunnah mu'akkadah, or even a mustahabb that has a special virtue (e.g., giving away your turn for the first row in prayer).
If you simply prefer another to do a same-value voluntary act, it may be permissible.
4
Apply the rule to known furūʿ
Work through examples: giving away your spot for taraweeh, letting someone else do the adhan, etc. Determine the hukm based on the value of the act given up.
Refer to the furuat in the qawida_panel below for more examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة : هل يُكره الإيثار بالقرب.
The third rule: Is giving preference to others in acts of devotion disliked?
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This short passage introduces the third rule of the second type: 'هل يُكره الإيثار بالقرب' (Is giving preference to others in acts of devotion disliked?). The text is incomplete, but the topic is whether it is makruh (disliked) to prefer others over oneself in matters of qurb (acts of worship or nearness to Allah), such as giving away one's turn for prayer or reward. The full discussion would likely examine the permissibility and ruling, and exceptions.

Key Terms
الإيثار (al-īthār): Giving preference to others over oneself in devotional matters.
القرب (al-qurb): Acts of devotion that bring one closer to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule introduced in this lesson, giving preference to others in acts of devotion (īthār fī al-qurb) is:
A) Always makruh
B) Never makruh
C) Disliked if it involves abandoning a personal virtue
D) Always mustahabb

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, īthār in worship is not always commendable; it is disliked if it means sacrificing a personal virtue in devotion.

The First Maxim: No Reward Without Intention – Foundation
Explaining the first qa'idah kulliya with its foundation and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'No reward except with intention' to distinguish between acts where intention is required for reward versus validity, and to identify the exceptions of Islam and kufr.

Core Idea
Intention determines reward, not always validity; many acts are valid without it.

This lesson unpacks the first universal maxim of Hanafi fiqh. The scholars ground this maxim in the famous hadith 'Actions are by intentions,' interpreting it as an implied statement about rewards and punishments. We will explore how the maxim governs acts of worship, with key exceptions that every mufti must know.

Guided Path
1
Interpret the Hadith of Intentions
Read the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' and understand the Hanafi interpretation that it implies a missing word (muqtada): 'the ruling of actions.' This explains why many acts are valid without intention.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Distinguish Between Otherworldly and Worldly Rulings
The hadith refers to otherworldly reward/punishment by consensus, not worldly validity. Recognize that intention is required for reward in all acts, but validity sometimes does not require it.
3
Identify Acts Where Intention is Required for Validity
From the text, prayer, zakah, fasting, hajj, and tayammum require intention for validity. List these and note the evidence: consensus or the verse 'وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين'.
Tayammum's proof is from the verse because the word 'tayammum' means 'intending'.
4
Identify Acts Where Intention is Not Required for Validity
Wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, removing light impurity, and washing the deceased do not require intention for validity. Intention only affects reward or lifting of obligation.
For washing the deceased, intention is for lifting the obligation from the living, not for the act's validity.
5
Note the Exceptions: Islam and Kufr
Islam is valid without intention (coerced Islam is valid), but kufr requires intention for its occurrence. Understand that these are exceptions to the general rule that intention is not needed for validity in some acts.
The jest of kufr is still kufr because the utterance itself indicates kufr, not because of intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول : القواعد الكلية
النوع الأول من هذه القواعد القاعدة الأولى : لا ثواب إلا بالنية
صرح به المشايخ في مواضع في الفقه أولها في الوضوء، سواء قلنا إنها شرط الصحة كما في الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج أو لا، كما في الوضوء والغسل، وعلى هذا قرروا حديث إنما الأعمال بالنيات (١) أنه من باب المقتضى، إذ لا يصح بدون تقدير لكثرة وجود الأعمال بدونها فقدروا مضافًا أي حكم الأعمال. وهو نوعان: أُخروي؛ هو الثواب واستحقاق العقاب. ودنيوي؛ وهو الصحة والفساد. وقد أُريد الأخروي بالإجماع، للإجماع على أنه لا ثواب ولا عقاب إلا بالنية، فانتفى الآخر أن يكون مرادًا، إما لأنه مشترك ولا عموم له، وإما لاندفاع الضرورة به من صحة الكلام، به فلا حاجة إلى الآخر. والثاني أوجه، لأن الأول لا يسلمه الخصم لأنه قائل بعموم المشترك، فحينئذ لا يدل على اشتراطها في الوسائل للصحة، ولا على المقاصد أيضًا. وفي بعض الكتب أن الوضوء الذي ليس بمنوي، ليس بمأمور به ولكنه مفتاح للصلاة.
إنما شرطت في العبادات بالإجماع، أو بآية وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مُخْلِصين له الدينَ حُنَفَاء [البيّنة : ٥] والأول أوجه لأن العبادة فيها بمعنى التوحيد بقرينة
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب بدء الوحي باب ۱ ومسلم في كتاب الإمارة حديث ١٥٥، وأبو داود في كتاب الطلاق باب ۱۱ وابن ماجه في كتاب الزهد باب ٢٦

عطف الصلاة والزكاة فلا تشترط في الوضوء والغسل ومسح الخُفَّين وإزالة النجاسة الخفيفة عن الثوب والبدن والمكان والأواني للصحة وأما اشتراطها في التيمم فلدلالة آيته عليها لأنه القصد، وأما غسل الميت؛ فقالوا لا تُشترط لصحة الصلاة عليه وتحصيل طهارته، وإنما هي شرط لإسقاط الفرض عن ذمة المكلفين. وتفرع عليه أن الغريق يُغسل ثلاثًا في قول أبي يوسف، وفي رواية عن محمد أنه إن نوى عند الإخراج من الماء يغسل مرتين وإن لم ينو فثلاثا وعنه يغسل مرة واحدة، كما في فتح القدير.
وأما في العبادات كلها فهي شرط صحتها إلا الإسلام، فإنه يصح بدونها بدليل قولهم : إن إسلام المكره صحيح ولا يكون مسلما بمجرد نية الإسلام بخلاف الكفر، كما سنبينه في بحث التروك، وأما الكفر فيشترط له النيَّة لقولهم: إن كفر المكره غير صحيح. وأما قولهم : إنه إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً يكفر. إنما هو باعتبار أن الكفر موجود، كما علم في الأصول من بحث الهزل فلا تصح صلاة مطلقا، ولو صلاة جنازة، إلا بها فرضًا أو واجبًا أو سُنَّة أو نفلاً. وإذا نوى قطعها لا يخرج عنها إلا بمناف ولو نوى الانتقال عنها إلى غيرها، فإن كانت الثانية غير الأولى وشرع بالتكبير، صار منتقلاً وإلا فلا ولا يصح اقتداء بإمام إلا بنية. وتصح الإمامة بدون نيتها خلافًا للكرخي وأبي حفص الكبير، كما في البناية إلا إذا صلى خلفه نساء، فإن اقتداءهن به بلا نيَّة الإمام للإمامة غير صحيح واستثنى بعضهم الجمعة والعيدين، وهو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة، ولو حلف ألا يؤم أحدًا فاقتدى به إنسان صح الاقتداء. وهل يحنث ؟ قال في الخانية : يحنث قضاء لا ديانة إلا إن أشهد قبل الشروع فلا يحنث قضاء وكذا لو أمّ الناس هذا الحالف في صلاة الجمعة صحت وحنث قضاء، ولا يحنث أصلاً إذا أمَّهم في صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة. ولو حلف ألا يؤم فلانا فأمَّ الناس ناويًا ألا يؤمه ويؤم غيره فاقتدى به فلان حنث وإن لم يعلم به (انتهى). ولكن لا ثواب له على الإمامة وسجود التلاوة كالصلاة. وكذا سجدة الشكر على قول من يراها مشروعة والمعتمد أن الخلاف في نيتها لا في الجواز وكذا سجود السهو، ولا تضره نية عدمه وقت السلام.
وأما النيَّة في الخطبة للجمعة فشرط لصحتها، حتى لو عطس بعد صعود المنبر فقال الحمد الله للعطاس غير قاصد لها لم تصح كما في فتح القدير وغيره. وخطبة العيدين كذلك لقولهم : يشترط لها ما يشترط لخطبة الجمعة سوى تقديم الخطبة. وأما الأذان فلا تشترط لصحته، وإنما هي شرط للثواب عليه. وأما استقبال القبلة،
The first art: The Universal Maxims. The first type of these maxims, the first maxim: No reward except with intention. The scholars have explicitly stated this in various places in fiqh, the first of which is in wudu, whether we say that intention is a condition for validity as in prayer, zakah, fasting, and hajj, or not as in wudu and ghusl. Based on this, they interpreted the hadith 'Verily actions are by intentions' as being of the category of implication (muqtada), because it is not valid without an implied addition due to the abundance of actions existing without intention; so they added an implied word, i.e., the ruling of actions. And it is of two types: otherworldly—which is reward and deserving of punishment; and worldly—which is validity and invalidity. The otherworldly is intended by consensus, because it is agreed that there is no reward or punishment except with intention, so the other is negated from being intended, either because the word is homonymous (mushtarak) and has no generality, or because necessity is fulfilled by the correctness of speech, so there is no need for the other. The second is more plausible, because the opponent does not concede the first as he holds that the homonym has generality; then it would not indicate its conditionality in means (wasa'il) for validity, nor concerning ends (maqasid). In some books it is stated that wudu that is not intended is not commanded but is a key to prayer. It is only made a condition in acts of worship by consensus, or by the verse 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, inclining to truth' [Bayyinah:5]. The first is more plausible because worship here means tawhid due to the conjunction with prayer and zakat. So intention is not a condition in wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, removing light impurity from garment, body, place, and vessels for validity. As for making it a condition in tayammum, it is by the indication of its verse because it means 'intending' (al-qasd). As for washing the deceased, they said it is not a condition for the validity of the prayer over him or for achieving his purification; rather it is a condition for lifting the obligation from the responsibility of the obligated. Derived from this: the drowned person is washed three times according to Abu Yusuf, and in a narration from Muhammad that if he intended upon removal from the water, he is washed twice; if he did not intend, then three times; and from him it is narrated once, as in Fath al-Qadir. As for all worships, it is a condition of their validity except Islam, for it is valid without it by the evidence of their statement: The Islam of one coerced is valid, and one does not become Muslim by mere intention of Islam, contrary to kufr, as we will explain in the discussion of omissions (al-taruk). As for kufr, intention is required for it due to their statement: The kufr of one coerced is not valid. As for their statement: If one speaks a word of kufr jokingly, he becomes a disbeliever—it is only because kufr exists, as known in usul from the discussion of jest. So no prayer is valid absolutely, even a funeral prayer, except with intention, whether fard, wajib, sunna, or nafl. If one intends to cut it, he does not exit from it except by something conflicting; and if he intends to transfer from it to another, if the second is different from the first and he commenced with takbir, he has transferred; otherwise not. Following an imam is not valid except with intention. The imamate is valid without its intention, contrary to al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir, as in al-Binaya, except if women pray behind him, then their following him without the imam's intention for imamate is not valid. Some exempted Friday and Eid prayers, and that is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. If one swore not to lead anyone in prayer, and then a person followed him, the following is valid. Does he break his oath? It is stated in al-Khaniyya: He breaks it judicially (qada') but not religiously (diyana), unless he took witnesses before starting, then he does not break it judicially. Similarly, if this swearer led people in Friday prayer, it is valid and he breaks it judicially. He does not break it at all if he led them in funeral prayer or prostration of recitation. If one swore not to lead a specific person, and then led people intending not to lead him but to lead others, and that specific person followed him, he breaks the oath even if he did not know him. But there is no reward for him for the imamate and prostration of recitation as in prayer. Similarly, the prostration of gratitude according to those who consider it legislated; the relied-upon view is that the difference is about its intention, not its permissibility. Similarly, prostration of forgetfulness, and the intention to not perform it at the time of salam does not harm him. As for intention in Friday sermon, it is a condition for its validity, so much so that if he sneezes after ascending the minbar and says 'alhamdulillah' for the sneeze without intending it, the sermon is not valid, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. The Eid sermon is the same because they say: Its conditions are the same as Friday sermon except for preceding the sermon. As for the adhan, intention is not a condition for its validity, but it is a condition for reward. As for facing the qibla,
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the first major maxim (qa'idah) of the first art: 'No reward except with intention' (لا ثواب إلا بالنية). The author begins by affirming the maxim as a universal principle in Hanafi fiqh, citing the famous hadith 'Verily actions are by intentions.' He explains that the hadith is understood as implying a missing element (muqtada) because actions often occur without intention; thus the hadith actually means 'the ruling of actions are by intentions.' This ruling is of two kinds: otherworldly (reward and punishment) and worldly (validity and invalidity). By consensus, the otherworldly is intended because no reward or punishment occurs without intention. The worldly aspect (validity) is not general, as intention is not required for validity in all acts—e.g., wudu and ghusl are valid without intention, though intention is required for reward. The author then details applications: intention is a condition for validity in prayers, zakat, fasting, hajj, and tayammum (by explicit Quranic indication), but not for wudu, ghusl, washing the deceased (where intention is only for the obligation upon the living), or removing impurity. For the drowning victim, the number of washes depends on intention per different Hanafi transmissions. The passage then covers exceptions: Islam (embracing Islam) is valid without intention (coerced Islam is valid), but kufr (blasphemy) requires intention for its validity. Jest in uttering kufr still counts as kufr. Intention is required for all prayers, including funeral prayer. Intention to exit prayer is not effective without an action; transferring to another prayer requires change of intent and takbir. Following an imam requires intention from the follower. The imam's own intention for imamate is not necessary for the validity of the congregation (contra al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir), except when women are present. Swearing about leading prayer involves nuanced rulings on judicial and religious consequences. Reward is absent for leading prayer or prostrations of recitation without proper intention. Intention is required for the Friday sermon's validity (even a sneeze response without intention invalidates it), similarly for Eid sermons. For adhan, intention is not required for validity but for reward. The passage ends abruptly while discussing facing the qibla. The authority of this text is from the Mutakhkhirin period (Fatawa works like Fath al-Qadir, al-Khulasa, al-Khaniyya).

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart; the purpose of the action.
ثواب (thawab): Reward from Allah in the Hereafter.
مقتضى (muqtada): Implication; an understood but omitted word in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements is true about the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات'?
A) It means that every action without intention is invalid.
B) It is interpreted as having an implied word 'ruling' (muqtada) and by consensus refers to otherworldly reward/punishment.
C) It applies only to acts of worship, not to daily actions.
D) It was revealed specifically about wudu.

Key Takeaway
The maxim separates reward from validity; intention is essential for reward but many acts are valid without it.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

Intention in Worship: Furuat and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Detailing the furuat of the first maxim across acts of worship and the imamate dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'No reward except with intention' to specific acts of worship (prayer, tayammum, imamate, sermon, etc.) and evaluate the intra-Hanafi difference on imamate.

Core Idea
Imam's intention is not required except with women; many acts need intention for validity, not only reward.

This lesson dives into the practical applications of the first universal maxim. We will examine a list of acts where intention is required for validity and those where it only affects reward. A key intra-Hanafi dispute about the imam's intention for imamate is also addressed, with the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
List Acts Requiring Intention for Validity
From the text, identify prayer, zakah, fasting, hajj, tayammum, Friday sermon, and Eid sermon. Note that intention is a condition (shart) for these acts.
Tayammum is proven by the verse because 'tayammum' means intending.
2
List Acts Where Intention is Not Required for Validity
Wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, removing impurity, washing the deceased, adhan, and imamate (for the imam's own intention) do not require intention for validity. Intention affects reward or lifting of obligation.
For washing the deceased, intention lifts the obligation from the living.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Imamate
The majority of Hanafis say the imam's intention is not required for the congregation's validity. Al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir disagree. Understand their proofs and the tarjih.
Exception: if women follow, the imam must intend imamate.
4
Apply to Oath Cases
If one swears not to lead, and someone follows, the following is valid but the oath may be broken judicially or religiously. Understand the nuances.
Reward for leading prayer is lost if the imam did not intend.
5
Summarize Other Furuat
Sermon, prostration of recitation, prostration of gratitude, and prostration of forgetfulness require intention for validity or reward. Note the specific rulings.
Sneezing in the sermon without intention invalidates it.

الفن الأول : القواعد الكلية
النوع الأول من هذه القواعد القاعدة الأولى : لا ثواب إلا بالنية
صرح به المشايخ في مواضع في الفقه أولها في الوضوء، سواء قلنا إنها شرط الصحة كما في الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج أو لا، كما في الوضوء والغسل، وعلى هذا قرروا حديث إنما الأعمال بالنيات (١) أنه من باب المقتضى، إذ لا يصح بدون تقدير لكثرة وجود الأعمال بدونها فقدروا مضافًا أي حكم الأعمال. وهو نوعان: أُخروي؛ هو الثواب واستحقاق العقاب. ودنيوي؛ وهو الصحة والفساد. وقد أُريد الأخروي بالإجماع، للإجماع على أنه لا ثواب ولا عقاب إلا بالنية، فانتفى الآخر أن يكون مرادًا، إما لأنه مشترك ولا عموم له، وإما لاندفاع الضرورة به من صحة الكلام، به فلا حاجة إلى الآخر. والثاني أوجه، لأن الأول لا يسلمه الخصم لأنه قائل بعموم المشترك، فحينئذ لا يدل على اشتراطها في الوسائل للصحة، ولا على المقاصد أيضًا. وفي بعض الكتب أن الوضوء الذي ليس بمنوي، ليس بمأمور به ولكنه مفتاح للصلاة.
إنما شرطت في العبادات بالإجماع، أو بآية وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مُخْلِصين له الدينَ حُنَفَاء [البيّنة : ٥] والأول أوجه لأن العبادة فيها بمعنى التوحيد بقرينة
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب بدء الوحي باب ۱ ومسلم في كتاب الإمارة حديث ١٥٥، وأبو داود في كتاب الطلاق باب ۱۱ وابن ماجه في كتاب الزهد باب ٢٦

عطف الصلاة والزكاة فلا تشترط في الوضوء والغسل ومسح الخُفَّين وإزالة النجاسة الخفيفة عن الثوب والبدن والمكان والأواني للصحة وأما اشتراطها في التيمم فلدلالة آيته عليها لأنه القصد، وأما غسل الميت؛ فقالوا لا تُشترط لصحة الصلاة عليه وتحصيل طهارته، وإنما هي شرط لإسقاط الفرض عن ذمة المكلفين. وتفرع عليه أن الغريق يُغسل ثلاثًا في قول أبي يوسف، وفي رواية عن محمد أنه إن نوى عند الإخراج من الماء يغسل مرتين وإن لم ينو فثلاثا وعنه يغسل مرة واحدة، كما في فتح القدير.
وأما في العبادات كلها فهي شرط صحتها إلا الإسلام، فإنه يصح بدونها بدليل قولهم : إن إسلام المكره صحيح ولا يكون مسلما بمجرد نية الإسلام بخلاف الكفر، كما سنبينه في بحث التروك، وأما الكفر فيشترط له النيَّة لقولهم: إن كفر المكره غير صحيح. وأما قولهم : إنه إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً يكفر. إنما هو باعتبار أن الكفر موجود، كما علم في الأصول من بحث الهزل فلا تصح صلاة مطلقا، ولو صلاة جنازة، إلا بها فرضًا أو واجبًا أو سُنَّة أو نفلاً. وإذا نوى قطعها لا يخرج عنها إلا بمناف ولو نوى الانتقال عنها إلى غيرها، فإن كانت الثانية غير الأولى وشرع بالتكبير، صار منتقلاً وإلا فلا ولا يصح اقتداء بإمام إلا بنية. وتصح الإمامة بدون نيتها خلافًا للكرخي وأبي حفص الكبير، كما في البناية إلا إذا صلى خلفه نساء، فإن اقتداءهن به بلا نيَّة الإمام للإمامة غير صحيح واستثنى بعضهم الجمعة والعيدين، وهو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة، ولو حلف ألا يؤم أحدًا فاقتدى به إنسان صح الاقتداء. وهل يحنث ؟ قال في الخانية : يحنث قضاء لا ديانة إلا إن أشهد قبل الشروع فلا يحنث قضاء وكذا لو أمّ الناس هذا الحالف في صلاة الجمعة صحت وحنث قضاء، ولا يحنث أصلاً إذا أمَّهم في صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة. ولو حلف ألا يؤم فلانا فأمَّ الناس ناويًا ألا يؤمه ويؤم غيره فاقتدى به فلان حنث وإن لم يعلم به (انتهى). ولكن لا ثواب له على الإمامة وسجود التلاوة كالصلاة. وكذا سجدة الشكر على قول من يراها مشروعة والمعتمد أن الخلاف في نيتها لا في الجواز وكذا سجود السهو، ولا تضره نية عدمه وقت السلام.
وأما النيَّة في الخطبة للجمعة فشرط لصحتها، حتى لو عطس بعد صعود المنبر فقال الحمد الله للعطاس غير قاصد لها لم تصح كما في فتح القدير وغيره. وخطبة العيدين كذلك لقولهم : يشترط لها ما يشترط لخطبة الجمعة سوى تقديم الخطبة. وأما الأذان فلا تشترط لصحته، وإنما هي شرط للثواب عليه. وأما استقبال القبلة،
The first art: The Universal Maxims. The first type of these maxims, the first maxim: No reward except with intention. The scholars have explicitly stated this in various places in fiqh, the first of which is in wudu, whether we say that intention is a condition for validity as in prayer, zakah, fasting, and hajj, or not as in wudu and ghusl. Based on this, they interpreted the hadith 'Verily actions are by intentions' as being of the category of implication (muqtada), because it is not valid without an implied addition due to the abundance of actions existing without intention; so they added an implied word, i.e., the ruling of actions. And it is of two types: otherworldly—which is reward and deserving of punishment; and worldly—which is validity and invalidity. The otherworldly is intended by consensus, because it is agreed that there is no reward or punishment except with intention, so the other is negated from being intended, either because the word is homonymous (mushtarak) and has no generality, or because necessity is fulfilled by the correctness of speech, so there is no need for the other. The second is more plausible, because the opponent does not concede the first as he holds that the homonym has generality; then it would not indicate its conditionality in means (wasa'il) for validity, nor concerning ends (maqasid). In some books it is stated that wudu that is not intended is not commanded but is a key to prayer. It is only made a condition in acts of worship by consensus, or by the verse 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, inclining to truth' [Bayyinah:5]. The first is more plausible because worship here means tawhid due to the conjunction with prayer and zakat. So intention is not a condition in wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, removing light impurity from garment, body, place, and vessels for validity. As for making it a condition in tayammum, it is by the indication of its verse because it means 'intending' (al-qasd). As for washing the deceased, they said it is not a condition for the validity of the prayer over him or for achieving his purification; rather it is a condition for lifting the obligation from the responsibility of the obligated. Derived from this: the drowned person is washed three times according to Abu Yusuf, and in a narration from Muhammad that if he intended upon removal from the water, he is washed twice; if he did not intend, then three times; and from him it is narrated once, as in Fath al-Qadir. As for all worships, it is a condition of their validity except Islam, for it is valid without it by the evidence of their statement: The Islam of one coerced is valid, and one does not become Muslim by mere intention of Islam, contrary to kufr, as we will explain in the discussion of omissions (al-taruk). As for kufr, intention is required for it due to their statement: The kufr of one coerced is not valid. As for their statement: If one speaks a word of kufr jokingly, he becomes a disbeliever—it is only because kufr exists, as known in usul from the discussion of jest. So no prayer is valid absolutely, even a funeral prayer, except with intention, whether fard, wajib, sunna, or nafl. If one intends to cut it, he does not exit from it except by something conflicting; and if he intends to transfer from it to another, if the second is different from the first and he commenced with takbir, he has transferred; otherwise not. Following an imam is not valid except with intention. The imamate is valid without its intention, contrary to al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir, as in al-Binaya, except if women pray behind him, then their following him without the imam's intention for imamate is not valid. Some exempted Friday and Eid prayers, and that is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. If one swore not to lead anyone in prayer, and then a person followed him, the following is valid. Does he break his oath? It is stated in al-Khaniyya: He breaks it judicially (qada') but not religiously (diyana), unless he took witnesses before starting, then he does not break it judicially. Similarly, if this swearer led people in Friday prayer, it is valid and he breaks it judicially. He does not break it at all if he led them in funeral prayer or prostration of recitation. If one swore not to lead a specific person, and then led people intending not to lead him but to lead others, and that specific person followed him, he breaks the oath even if he did not know him. But there is no reward for him for the imamate and prostration of recitation as in prayer. Similarly, the prostration of gratitude according to those who consider it legislated; the relied-upon view is that the difference is about its intention, not its permissibility. Similarly, prostration of forgetfulness, and the intention to not perform it at the time of salam does not harm him. As for intention in Friday sermon, it is a condition for its validity, so much so that if he sneezes after ascending the minbar and says 'alhamdulillah' for the sneeze without intending it, the sermon is not valid, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. The Eid sermon is the same because they say: Its conditions are the same as Friday sermon except for preceding the sermon. As for the adhan, intention is not a condition for its validity, but it is a condition for reward. As for facing the qibla,
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the first major maxim (qa'idah) of the first art: 'No reward except with intention' (لا ثواب إلا بالنية). The author begins by affirming the maxim as a universal principle in Hanafi fiqh, citing the famous hadith 'Verily actions are by intentions.' He explains that the hadith is understood as implying a missing element (muqtada) because actions often occur without intention; thus the hadith actually means 'the ruling of actions are by intentions.' This ruling is of two kinds: otherworldly (reward and punishment) and worldly (validity and invalidity). By consensus, the otherworldly is intended because no reward or punishment occurs without intention. The worldly aspect (validity) is not general, as intention is not required for validity in all acts—e.g., wudu and ghusl are valid without intention, though intention is required for reward. The author then details applications: intention is a condition for validity in prayers, zakat, fasting, hajj, and tayammum (by explicit Quranic indication), but not for wudu, ghusl, washing the deceased (where intention is only for the obligation upon the living), or removing impurity. For the drowning victim, the number of washes depends on intention per different Hanafi transmissions. The passage then covers exceptions: Islam (embracing Islam) is valid without intention (coerced Islam is valid), but kufr (blasphemy) requires intention for its validity. Jest in uttering kufr still counts as kufr. Intention is required for all prayers, including funeral prayer. Intention to exit prayer is not effective without an action; transferring to another prayer requires change of intent and takbir. Following an imam requires intention from the follower. The imam's own intention for imamate is not necessary for the validity of the congregation (contra al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir), except when women are present. Swearing about leading prayer involves nuanced rulings on judicial and religious consequences. Reward is absent for leading prayer or prostrations of recitation without proper intention. Intention is required for the Friday sermon's validity (even a sneeze response without intention invalidates it), similarly for Eid sermons. For adhan, intention is not required for validity but for reward. The passage ends abruptly while discussing facing the qibla. The authority of this text is from the Mutakhkhirin period (Fatawa works like Fath al-Qadir, al-Khulasa, al-Khaniyya).

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart; the purpose of the action.
إمامة (imamah): Leading prayer in congregation.
خطبة (khutbah): Sermon, especially Friday sermon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person leads the funeral prayer without intending imamate?
A) The prayer is invalid.
B) The prayer is valid, but the imam loses reward.
C) The prayer is valid only if the followers intended the imam.
D) The prayer is invalid if there are women present.

Key Takeaway
Imamate is valid without the imam's intention, except when women are present; this is the mufta bihi.

Intention in Purification Acts: A Hanafi Qa'ida with Furu'
Rulings on intention in purification acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-niyyah shart fi ba'd al-'ibadat duna ba'd' to distinguish when intention is required for validity and when it affects obligation, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute on washing the drowned person.

Core Idea
Intention is not required for validity in most purifications except tayammum; for washing the deceased, it affects obligation removal.

The role of intention (niyyah) in purification acts is often misunderstood. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a subtle Hanafi qawida that distinguishes between acts where intention is a condition for validity, and acts where it is only a condition for the obligation being fulfilled. Through the example of tayammum and the case of washing the deceased—especially the drowned person—the passage illustrates how the qawida generates practical furu' and even reveals an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its scope
Identify the general rule: intention is a condition in some acts of worship but not others. Note the chapters it governs (taharah, janazah).
2
Classify the furu'
For each case (wudu, ghusl, tayammum, washing deceased), determine whether intention is a condition for validity or for obligation. Use the passage to categorize.
3
Analyze the istithna'
Tayammum is the exception: intention is required for validity. Why? Because the Quranic verse indicates it. Understand the dalil.
Quran 5:6
4
Examine the intra-madhhab dispute
On the drowned person, note the differing views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. How does the qawida apply to this furu'? The dispute hinges on whether intention at removal affects the number of washes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عطف الصلاة والزكاة فلا تشترط في الوضوء والغسل ومسح الخُفَّين وإزالة النجاسة الخفيفة عن الثوب والبدن والمكان والأواني للصحة وأما اشتراطها في التيمم فلدلالة آيته عليها لأنه القصد، وأما غسل الميت؛ فقالوا لا تُشترط لصحة الصلاة عليه وتحصيل طهارته، وإنما هي شرط لإسقاط الفرض عن ذمة المكلفين. وتفرع عليه أن الغريق يُغسل ثلاثًا في قول أبي يوسف، وفي رواية عن محمد أنه إن نوى عند الإخراج من الماء يغسل مرتين وإن لم ينو فثلاثا وعنه يغسل مرة واحدة، كما في فتح القدير.
And attached to prayer and zakat: it (intention) is not a condition in wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, removing light impurity from garment, body, place, and vessels for validity. As for it being a condition in tayammum, it is because its verse indicates it, for it is the intended act. As for washing the deceased, they said it is not a condition for the validity of prayer upon him or achieving his purification; rather it is a condition for removing the obligation from the liability of those obligated. Derived from that: the drowned person is washed three times according to Abu Yusuf, and in a narration from Muhammad that if he intended at the time of removal from water, he is washed twice; if he did not intend, then three times; and from him (another narration): he is washed once, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage addresses the role of intention (niyyah) in acts of purification. It states that intention is not a condition for the validity of wudu, ghusl, wiping over khuffs, or removing light impurities. However, it is a condition for tayammum because the Quranic verse explicitly mentions it, as tayammum is an act that inherently requires intent. For washing the deceased, the scholars clarify that intention is not a condition for the validity of the funeral prayer or for the purification of the deceased; rather, it is only a condition for the obligation being lifted from the living. An application of this is the case of a drowned person: according to Abu Yusuf, he is washed three times; according to Muhammad, if he intended at the time of removal, he is washed twice; otherwise three times; another narration from Muhammad says once. This illustrates the furuat (branches) of the general rule.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention of the heart to perform an act of worship or to execute a legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following acts of purification is intention a condition for validity according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wudu
B) Tayammum
C) Ghusl
D) Removing light impurity from a garment

Key Takeaway
Intention is not required for validity of wudu, ghusl, or impurity removal, but is required for tayammum; for washing the deceased, it affects whether the obligation is fulfilled, not the validity.

Intention in Khutbah, Adhan, Qibla, and Satr: A Qawida with Furu'at
Rulings on intention in Friday/Eid khutbah, adhan, qibla, and satr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida that intention is required for the validity of the khutbah but not for adhan, facing qibla, or covering the private parts, and to identify the reward principle for mistaken acts of worship.

Core Idea
Intention conditions only the khutbah's validity; other prayer-associated acts do not require intention for validity.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a series of rulings on the role of intention in actions connected to prayer. While intention is essential for the validity of the Friday and Eid khutbahs, it is not required for the adhan, facing the qibla, or covering the private parts. The passage also introduces a broader principle: reward is based on intention, not the objective validity of the act. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and their application as furu' of a qawida.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida's scope
Recognize that the qawida covers acts surrounding prayer: khutbah, adhan, qibla, satr. Note that intention is a condition for validity only for the khutbah.
The khutbah includes both Friday and Eid.
2
Distinguish validity conditions from reward conditions
For khutbah, intention is a condition for validity; for adhan, only for reward; for qibla and satr, no intention needed for validity.
This distinction is crucial in iftaa.
3
Apply the reward principle to mistaken worship
Even if an act is invalid due to an honest mistake (e.g., praying without wudu), reward is still given for the intention. This is a separate maxim.
The passage promises further investigation of this principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النيَّة في الخطبة للجمعة فشرط لصحتها، حتى لو عطس بعد صعود المنبر فقال الحمد الله للعطاس غير قاصد لها لم تصح كما في فتح القدير وغيره. وخطبة العيدين كذلك لقولهم : يشترط لها ما يشترط لخطبة الجمعة سوى تقديم الخطبة. وأما الأذان فلا تشترط لصحته، وإنما هي شرط للثواب عليه. وأما استقبال القبلة، فشرط الجرجاني لصحته النيَّة والصحيح خلافه كما في المبسوط، وحمل بعضهم الأول على ما إذا كان يصلي في الصحراء، والثاني على ما إذا كان يصلي إلى محراب، كذا في البناية. وأما ستر العورة فلا تشترط لصحته، ولم أرَ فيه خلافا. ولا تشترط للثواب صحة العبادة، بل يُثاب على نيَّته، وإن كانت فاسدة بغير تعمّده كما لو صلَّى محدثًا على ظن طهارته، وسيأتي تحقيقه.
As for intention in the Friday khutbah, it is a condition for its validity. So if one sneezes after ascending the minbar and says al-hamdu lillah for the sneeze without intending it (the khutbah), it is not valid, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. The khutbah of the two Eids is also like that, for they said: it requires what is required for the Friday khutbah except for the precedence of the khutbah. As for the adhan, intention is not a condition for its validity, rather it is a condition for reward upon it. As for facing the qibla, al-Jurjani made intention a condition for its validity; the correct view is contrary to this, as in al-Mabsut. Some interpreted the first view for when one prays in the desert, and the second for when one prays toward a mihrab, as in al-Binaya. As for covering the private parts, intention is not a condition for its validity, and I have not seen any disagreement on that. And the validity of the act of worship is not a condition for reward; rather one is rewarded for his intention, even if the act is invalid without his deliberate fault, like if one prayed without wudu thinking he was pure; and its investigation will come.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage discusses the requirement of intention in acts connected to prayer but not themselves worship. For the Friday khutbah, intention is a condition for its validity; if someone sneezes on the minbar and says 'al-hamdu lillah' merely for the sneeze, not intending the khutbah, it does not count. The same applies to the two Eid khutbahs. For the adhan, intention is not required for its validity but only for reward. For facing the qibla, there is a disagreement: al-Jurjani required intention for the validity of facing the qibla, but the correct Hanafi position rejects that; some reconcile by saying intention is needed in the desert but not when praying toward a mihrab. Covering the private parts requires no intention. Finally, the text notes a broader principle: even if an act of worship is invalid due to a mistake on the part of the worshipper (e.g., praying without wudu thinking he was pure), he still receives reward for his intention. This teaches that intention is the basis for reward, not the correctness of the act.

Key Terms
خطبة (khutbah): The sermon delivered on Friday or Eid prayers.
أذان (adhan): The call to prayer.
نية (niyyah): Intention, the mental resolve to perform an act for the sake of Allah.
شرط (shart): Condition; something whose absence renders the act invalid.
ثواب (thawab): Reward from Allah for righteous actions and intentions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, intention is a condition for the validity of which of the following?
A) Adhan
B) Covering private parts
C) Friday khutbah
D) Facing qibla in the desert

Key Takeaway
Intention is only a condition for the validity of the khutbah; for adhan, qibla, and satr it is not required for validity, but intention is always the basis for reward.

Exceptions and Conditions for Intention in Zakat
Exceptions and details on intention in zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptions to the requirement of intention in zakat and apply the conditions for intention to trade goods and livestock according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Intention's requirement in zakat has exceptions: full charity waives it; for trade goods, intention must be concurrent.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair expands on a key maxim: intention is required for zakat on trade goods, but there are important exceptions. You will learn when the obligation drops entirely, when it is disputed, and how intention must be timed and directed for goods and livestock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main maxim
Recognise that intention (niyyat al-tijarah) is generally required for zakat on merchandise (`urud). This is the rule from which exceptions are drawn.
The maxim is: نية التجارة شرط لزكاة العروض.
2
Examine the exception for charity of entire nisab
Note that if a person gives away their entire zakatable wealth without intending zakat, the obligation falls. This is because the wealth no longer exists, so no zakat is due.
3
Analyse the dispute on partial charity
There is a difference of opinion among the Hanafi imams about whether giving part of the nisab as charity also drops the zakat on that portion. The text reports the dispute without settling it.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the condition for trade goods
For `urud al-tijarah, the intention for trade must be concurrent with the acquisition (e.g., at the time of purchase). Conditional or future intention does not suffice.
Examples illustrate that buying for personal use with a conditional profit intention does not trigger trade zakat.
5
Apply the rule to special cases
Intention for trade does not affect zakat on crops from any land type (tithe, kharaj, leased, borrowed). Also, property acquired without exchange (gift, mahr, etc.) cannot become trade goods by mere intention.
For sa'imah, the primary purpose matters: if intended for milk/offspring, sa'imah zakat applies; if for trade, trade zakat requires concurrent intention; if for riding/loading/eating, no zakat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج عن اشتراطها لها ما إذا تصدق بجميع النصاب بلا نية فإن الفرض يسقط عنه واختلفوا في سقوط زكاة البعض إذا تصدق به، قالوا: وتشترط نية التجارة العروض ولا بد أن تكون مقارنة للتجارة، فلو اشترى شيئًا للقنية ناويا أنه إن وجد ربحا باعه، ولا زكاة عليه ولو نوى التجارة فيما خرج من أرضه العشرية أو الخراجية أو المستأجرة أو المستعارة لا زكاة عليه ولو قارنت ما ليس بدل مال بمال، كالهبة والصدقة والخلع والمهر والوصية لا تصح على الصحيح. وفي السائمة لا بد من قصد إسامتها للدرّ والنسل أكثر الحول فإن قصد به التجارة ففيها زكاة التجارة إن قارنت الشراء، وإن قصد به الحمل أو الركوب أو الأكل فلا زكاة أصلاً.
And removed from its (intention's) requirement for zakat is the case when one gives all the nisab as charity without intention: then the obligation falls from him. And they differed about the fall of zakat on part of it if he gives it as charity. They said: Intention for trade is required for merchandise, and it must be concurrent with the trade. So if one buys something for personal use intending that if he finds profit he will sell it, there is no zakat on him. And if he intends trade on what comes out of his tithe land, kharaj land, leased land, or borrowed land, there is no zakat on him. And if it is concurrent (intention) with that which is not a exchange of property for property, such as gift, charity, khul', mahr, and bequest, it is not valid according to the correct view. And regarding grazing livestock, it is necessary to intend to let them graze for milk and offspring for most of the year. If he intends trade with them, then zakat of trade applies if the intention coincided with the purchase. And if he intends them for riding, loading, or eating, there is no zakat at all.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents exceptions and details regarding intention in zakat. First, if a person gives away his entire nisab (wealth subject to zakat) as charity without intending zakat, the obligation of zakat is waived. There is disagreement about whether giving part of the nisab as charity also drops the zakat on that part. Next, for trade goods, intention for trade must accompany the acquisition; conditional or future intentions do not trigger zakat. Various examples illustrate that zakat on trade requires a specific intent at the time of purchase. For crops from different land types, even if one intends trade, no zakat is due because the zakat on land produce is based on the land type, not intention. For assets acquired through non-exchange transactions (gift, charity, khul', mahr, will), intention for trade cannot make them subject to zakat. Finally, for grazing livestock (sa'imah), the animal must be intended for milk and offspring for most of the year; if intended for trade, trade zakat applies if the intention coincided with purchase; if intended for riding, loading, or eating, no zakat at all.

Key Terms
نية التجارة (niyyat al-tijarah): Intention for trade that must be concurrent with the acquisition of merchandise to subject it to trade zakat.
سائمة (sa'imah): Grazing livestock that are left to graze for most of the year, subject to zakat if intended for milk and offspring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person gives part of his nisab as charity without intention, what is the ruling?
A) The zakat obligation falls for that part.
B) There is a disagreement among scholars.
C) The zakat obligation remains for that part.
D) The entire nisab becomes exempt from zakat.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intention for zakat on trade goods must be concurrent with acquisition, and this requirement does not apply to property obtained without exchange.

Intention in Fasting, Hajj, Itikaf, Kaffarat, and Udhiyah
Qawida with furu' applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-niyyah shart' to determine when intention is required for acts of worship and evaluate derived furu' cases correctly.

Core Idea
Intention is a condition; suspending it on God's will is valid because intention is a heart act, not speech.

This lesson unpacks a core Hanafi maxim: intention is a condition for fasting, hajj, itikaf, expiations, and sacrifices. The passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir not only states the maxim but also examines critical edge cases—such as suspending the intention, the timing for udhiyah, and the effect of compensation. Mastering these details is essential for issuing accurate fatwa in these acts.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and identify its scope
Read the maxim: 'al-niyyah shart fi al-sawm wa al-hajj...' (intention is a condition for fasting, hajj, etc.). Note the acts covered: fasting, hajj/umrah, itikaf, kaffarat, udhiyah.
The maxim is general (kulliya) but applies differently to each act.
2
Analyze the furu' on fasting
The passage says suspending intention on God's will (mashi'ah) is valid because intention is not a statement (qawl). Understand why: statements can be invalidated by ta'liq, but heart intentions cannot.
This applies equally to fard, sunna, and nafl fasts.
3
Examine the furu' on hajj and umrah
Intention is required for both fard and nafl. Vowed hajj is treated like fard. Note the special case of vowing 'Hajj al-Islam': if one has already performed it, the vow may not obligate another? The text is ambiguous.
The key is that intention is needed at the start of the act.
4
Apply to itikaf and kaffarat
Intention is required for itikaf whether wajib, sunna, or nafl. For kaffarat (freeing, fasting, feeding), intention is a condition. Note that these are compensatory acts; intention differentiates them from voluntary acts.
For kaffarat, the intention must be to fulfill the expiation.
5
Deduce the rule for udhiyah and its derivative case
Intention is required at purchase, not at slaughter. Then work through the fur': if someone else slaughters without permission, the owner's action (taking the meat without claiming compensation) indicates the intention was satisfied.
This shows that the legal effect of intention can be evidenced by subsequent conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النية في الصوم، فشرط صحته لكل يوم، ولو علقها بالمشيئة صحت لأنها إنما تبطل الأقوال، والنيَّة ليست منها والفرض والسُّنَّة والنفل في أصلها سواء. وأما الحج؛ فهي شرط صحته أيضًا، فرضًا كان أو نفلاً. والعمرة كذلك، ولا تكون إلا سنة والمنذور كالفرض، ولو نذر حجة الإسلام لا يلزمه إلا حجة الإسلام كما لو نذر إلا ضحية والقضاء في الكل كالأداء من جهة أصل النية. وأما الاعتكاف ؛ فهي شرط صحته واجبًا كان أو سُنَّة أو نفلاً. وأما الكفارات ؛ فالنيَّة شرط صحتها، عِتقا أو صيامًا أو إطعاما . وأما الضحايا فلا بد فيها من النيَّة، لكن عند الشراء لا عند الذبح وتفرّع عليه أنه لو اشتراها بنية الأضحية فذبحها غيره بلا إذن فإن أخذها مذبوحة ولم يضمنه أجزأته، وإن ضمنه لا
As for intention in fasting, it is a condition for its validity for each day. And if he suspends it on the will of God, it is valid because (the suspension) only invalidates statements, and intention is not from them. The obligatory, sunna, and nafl are equal in their essence. As for hajj, it is a condition for its validity as well, whether fard or nafl. Umrah is also like that, and it is only a sunna. The vowed (hajj) is like fard. If one vows the Hajj of Islam, only the Hajj of Islam is incumbent upon him, just as if he vowed a sacrifice. And making up (qada) in all cases is like the original performance with respect to the foundation of intention. As for itikaf, it is a condition for its validity, whether wajib, sunna, or nafl. As for expiations (kaffarat), intention is a condition for their validity, whether freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding. As for sacrifices (udhiyah), intention is necessary, but at the time of purchase, not at the time of slaughter. And derived from that: if one buys it with the intention of sacrifice, and someone else slaughters it without permission, if he takes it slaughtered and does not hold him liable (for the value), it suffices; and if he holds him liable, it does not.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of intention as a condition for various acts of worship. For fasting, intention is required for each day. Suspending the intention on God's will (e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow if God wills') is valid because intention is an act of the heart, not a statement; it does not invalidate the intention. The rule applies equally to obligatory, sunna, and nafl fasts. For hajj and umrah, intention is required whether they are obligatory or voluntary; a vowed hajj (nazr) is like the obligatory hajj. If someone vows to perform the 'Hajj of Islam', it only obligates the Islamic hajj (if he hasn't performed it), similar to vowing a specific sacrifice. For itikaf (retreat), intention is a condition whether it is wajib, sunna, or nafl. For expiations (kaffarat), intention is required whether the expiation is freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding. For udhiyah (sacrifice), intention is required at the time of purchase, not at slaughter. A derived ruling: if a person buys an animal intending it for sacrifice, and someone else slaughters it without permission, if the owner takes the slaughtered animal without demanding compensation from the slaughterer, it suffices for the sacrifice; if he demands compensation, it does not suffice.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Heart's intention required for validity of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is intention required for the sacrifice (udhiyah)?
A) At the time of slaughter only
B) At the time of purchase only
C) Both at purchase and slaughter
D) It is not required

Key Takeaway
Intention is a condition for these acts, but its timing varies: for udhiyah, it is required at purchase; for others, at the start of the act.

Intention in Zakat: The Dispute on Forceful Collection
States the necessity of intention for zakat and discusses internal disagreement on forceful collection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intention is necessary for valid zakat, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on forceful collection, and apply the mufta bihi position in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
Zakat requires intention; forceful collection without the owner's free choice does not fulfill the obligation.

This passage affirms that intention is a condition for zakat's validity. It then presents a weak intra-Hanafi view that the imam's forceful taking suffices, and rejects it, emphasizing the necessity of the owner's voluntary choice. The relied-upon method is imprisonment to compel payment with intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage begins by stating that zakat is not valid without intention (niyyah). This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh.
Intention is a condition for all acts of worship, including zakat.
2
Locate the opposing view
Al-Qadi al-Asbijabi argued that if a person refuses to pay zakat, the imam can take it forcefully and it suffices because the imam has authority (wilayah).
This is a weak view within the Hanafi school.
3
Identify the Hanafi response
The relied-upon position rejects this: forceful taking does not count as zakat because it lacks the owner's choice (ikhtiyar). Instead, the imam should imprison the refuser to compel voluntary payment.
This is quoted from al-Muhit, a prominent Hanafi text.
4
Examine the proof
The proof is that intention is a condition for zakat validation. Forceful taking lacks intention because the owner did not freely choose to pay.
The absence of ikhtiyar invalidates the act.
5
Determine the thamarah
If the imam takes forcefully, the obligation remains; the payer must pay again voluntarily to fulfill his duty.
This is the practical consequence for a mufti advising on such a case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزكاة؛ فلا يصح أداؤها إلا بالنيَّة وعلى هذا فما ذكره القاضي الأسبيجابي : أن من امتنع عن أدائها أخذها الإمام كرها ووضعها في أهلها، وتجزيه لأن للإمام ولاية ،أخذها فقام أخذه مقام دفع المالك باختياره، فهو ضعيف. والمعتمد في المذهب عدم الإجزاء كرها قال في المحيط : ومن امتنع عن أداء الزكاة فالساعي لا يأخذ منه كرها، ولو أخذ لا يقع عن الزكاة لكونها بلا اختيار، ولكن يجبره بالحبس ليؤدي بنفسه . (انتهى). · وخرج عن اشتراطها لها ما إذا تصدق بجميع النصاب بلا نية فإن الفرض يسقط عنه واختلفوا في سقوط زكاة البعض إذا تصدق به، قالوا: وتشترط نية التجارة العروض ولا بد أن تكون مقارنة للتجارة، فلو اشترى شيئًا للقنية ناويا أنه إن وجد ربحا باعه، ولا زكاة عليه ولو نوى التجارة فيما خرج من أرضه العشرية أو الخراجية أو المستأجرة أو المستعارة لا زكاة عليه ولو قارنت ما ليس بدل مال بمال، كالهبة والصدقة والخلع والمهر والوصية لا تصح على الصحيح. وفي السائمة لا بد من قصد إسامتها للدرّ والنسل أكثر الحول فإن قصد به التجارة ففيها زكاة التجارة إن قارنت الشراء، وإن قصد به الحمل أو الركوب أو الأكل فلا زكاة أصلاً.
As for zakat, its payment is not valid except with intention. Based on this, what al-Qadi al-Asbijabi mentioned—that if someone refuses to pay it, the imam takes it forcefully and distributes it to the deserving, and it suffices because the imam has authority to take it, so his taking stands in place of the owner's voluntary payment—is weak. The relied-upon position in the madhhab is that it does not suffice if taken forcefully. Al-Muhit states: If someone refuses to pay zakat, the collector (sa'i) should not take it forcefully; if he does, it does not count as zakat because it is without choice. Rather, he should compel him by imprisonment so that he pays it himself. (End). And an exception from the requirement of intention is when one gives away the entire nisab as charity without intention; the obligation is dropped. They differed on whether zakat on part of the nisab is dropped if one gives it away. They said: Intention of trade is required for trade goods, and it must coincide with the transaction. If one buys something for personal use intending to sell it if he finds profit, there is no zakat on it. If one intends trade for produce from his 'ushri, kharaji, rented, or borrowed land, there is no zakat. If it coincides with something that is not a monetary exchange, like gift, charity, khul', mahr, or bequest, it is not valid according to the correct view. For grazing livestock (sa'imah), one must intend to pasture them for milk and offspring for most of the year; if one intends trade, then trade zakat applies if it coincides with purchase; if one intends for riding, carrying, or eating, then no zakat at all.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on intention in zakat. It begins by affirming that intention is essential for the validity of zakat payment. It then critiques a contrary view by al-Qadi al-Asbijabi, who held that the imam can forcefully take zakat and it would suffice. The Hanafi madhhab rejects this, as clear from al-Muhit: forceful collection does not fulfill the obligation; instead, the imam should imprison the person to make him pay willingly. Next, it discusses an exception: if one gives away the entire nisab as charity without intending zakat, the obligation is dropped entirely. There is disagreement on whether giving away part of the nisab has a similar effect. The passage then addresses intention for trade goods ('urud al-tijarah): the intention of trade must be present at the time of purchase. If one buys an item for personal use but later decides to sell if profit appears, no zakat is due. Similarly, produce from land (whether 'ushri, kharaji, rented, or borrowed) does not incur zakat even with trade intention. If a transaction is not a monetary exchange (e.g., gift, khul'), intention does not make it a trade. For grazing livestock, the owner must intend to keep them for milk and offspring for most of the year; if he intends trade at purchase, zakat is on the trade value; if he intends other uses, no zakat.

Key Terms
الساعي (al-sa'i): The official collector of zakat appointed by the imam.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention, a condition for the validity of acts of worship including zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in al-Muhit, what should the sa'i do if someone refuses to pay zakat?
A) Take it forcefully and distribute it.
B) Imprison him until he pays voluntarily.
C) Leave him alone as it is his choice.
D) Take half forcefully as a fine.

Key Takeaway
Intention is essential for zakat; the imam's forceful taking without the owner's willingness does not fulfill the obligation.

8 min
Intention in Zakat: Exceptions and Special Cases
Explains exceptions to intention requirement and special rules for trade goods and livestock.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exception of giving away the entire nisab without intention, and apply the rules for trade goods and grazing livestock concerning intention.

Core Idea
Giving all nisab as charity drops zakat; trade intention must coincide with purchase; livestock intention determines zakat type.

This passage covers exceptions to the general rule that zakat requires intention. It first mentions that giving away the entire nisab as charity without niyyah drops the obligation. It then details the crucial timing of intention for trade goods and the specific intention required for grazing livestock. These rules prevent common errors in zakat calculation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the exception for entire nisab
The passage states that if someone gives away all of their zakatable wealth (nisab) as charity without intending zakat, the zakat obligation is dropped. There is a disagreement on giving part of the nisab.
This exception is specific to giving away the entire nisab.
2
Understand the trade goods rule
For trade goods ('urud al-tijarah), the intention of trade must be present at the time of purchase. If one buys for personal use but later decides to sell, no zakat is due on the goods.
Zakat on trade goods is based on the intention at acquisition.
3
Apply to non-monetary exchanges
If the transaction is not a monetary exchange (like gift, khul', mahr), even with trade intention, it does not count as trade goods for zakat.
The nature of the transaction affects the applicability.
4
Analyze the livestock rule
For grazing livestock (sa'imah), the owner must intend to keep them for milk and offspring for most of the year. If trade intention is present at purchase, trade zakat applies; if for riding, carrying, or eating, no zakat at all.
The intention determines whether zakat is due and what type.
5
Synthesize the principles
In each case, the timing and nature of intention are decisive. The mufti must verify the payer's intention at key moments to rule correctly.
These rules show that intention is not just a formality but has specific legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزكاة؛ فلا يصح أداؤها إلا بالنيَّة وعلى هذا فما ذكره القاضي الأسبيجابي : أن من امتنع عن أدائها أخذها الإمام كرها ووضعها في أهلها، وتجزيه لأن للإمام ولاية ،أخذها فقام أخذه مقام دفع المالك باختياره، فهو ضعيف. والمعتمد في المذهب عدم الإجزاء كرها قال في المحيط : ومن امتنع عن أداء الزكاة فالساعي لا يأخذ منه كرها، ولو أخذ لا يقع عن الزكاة لكونها بلا اختيار، ولكن يجبره بالحبس ليؤدي بنفسه . (انتهى). · وخرج عن اشتراطها لها ما إذا تصدق بجميع النصاب بلا نية فإن الفرض يسقط عنه واختلفوا في سقوط زكاة البعض إذا تصدق به، قالوا: وتشترط نية التجارة العروض ولا بد أن تكون مقارنة للتجارة، فلو اشترى شيئًا للقنية ناويا أنه إن وجد ربحا باعه، ولا زكاة عليه ولو نوى التجارة فيما خرج من أرضه العشرية أو الخراجية أو المستأجرة أو المستعارة لا زكاة عليه ولو قارنت ما ليس بدل مال بمال، كالهبة والصدقة والخلع والمهر والوصية لا تصح على الصحيح. وفي السائمة لا بد من قصد إسامتها للدرّ والنسل أكثر الحول فإن قصد به التجارة ففيها زكاة التجارة إن قارنت الشراء، وإن قصد به الحمل أو الركوب أو الأكل فلا زكاة أصلاً.
As for zakat, its payment is not valid except with intention. Based on this, what al-Qadi al-Asbijabi mentioned—that if someone refuses to pay it, the imam takes it forcefully and distributes it to the deserving, and it suffices because the imam has authority to take it, so his taking stands in place of the owner's voluntary payment—is weak. The relied-upon position in the madhhab is that it does not suffice if taken forcefully. Al-Muhit states: If someone refuses to pay zakat, the collector (sa'i) should not take it forcefully; if he does, it does not count as zakat because it is without choice. Rather, he should compel him by imprisonment so that he pays it himself. (End). And an exception from the requirement of intention is when one gives away the entire nisab as charity without intention; the obligation is dropped. They differed on whether zakat on part of the nisab is dropped if one gives it away. They said: Intention of trade is required for trade goods, and it must coincide with the transaction. If one buys something for personal use intending to sell it if he finds profit, there is no zakat on it. If one intends trade for produce from his 'ushri, kharaji, rented, or borrowed land, there is no zakat. If it coincides with something that is not a monetary exchange, like gift, charity, khul', mahr, or bequest, it is not valid according to the correct view. For grazing livestock (sa'imah), one must intend to pasture them for milk and offspring for most of the year; if one intends trade, then trade zakat applies if it coincides with purchase; if one intends for riding, carrying, or eating, then no zakat at all.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on intention in zakat. It begins by affirming that intention is essential for the validity of zakat payment. It then critiques a contrary view by al-Qadi al-Asbijabi, who held that the imam can forcefully take zakat and it would suffice. The Hanafi madhhab rejects this, as clear from al-Muhit: forceful collection does not fulfill the obligation; instead, the imam should imprison the person to make him pay willingly. Next, it discusses an exception: if one gives away the entire nisab as charity without intending zakat, the obligation is dropped entirely. There is disagreement on whether giving away part of the nisab has a similar effect. The passage then addresses intention for trade goods ('urud al-tijarah): the intention of trade must be present at the time of purchase. If one buys an item for personal use but later decides to sell if profit appears, no zakat is due. Similarly, produce from land (whether 'ushri, kharaji, rented, or borrowed) does not incur zakat even with trade intention. If a transaction is not a monetary exchange (e.g., gift, khul'), intention does not make it a trade. For grazing livestock, the owner must intend to keep them for milk and offspring for most of the year; if he intends trade at purchase, zakat is on the trade value; if he intends other uses, no zakat.

Key Terms
عروض التجارة (urud al-tijarah): Goods purchased with the intention of resale for profit.
السائمة (al-sa'imah): Grazing livestock that are pastured for most of the year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone gives away the entire nisab as charity without intending zakat, what happens to the zakat obligation according to the passage?
A) It is still due.
B) It is dropped.
C) It becomes a sunnah.
D) It must be repaid with extra.

Key Takeaway
Giving all nisab as charity drops zakat; trade intention must be at purchase; livestock intention determines zakat type.
