Verbalization is Required in Vows, Endowments, and Commencement of Rituals
Exceptions to the rule that intention is sufficient

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptions to the Hanafi principle that intention suffices without verbalization, and apply the rule to cases of nadhr, waqf, takbir, and talbiyah.

Core Idea
Intention alone is not enough for vows, endowments, starting prayer, and ihram — verbalization is required.

The second grand maxim of Hanafi fiqh states that intention in the heart suffices without uttering it aloud for all acts of worship. However, this rule has well-known exceptions where verbalization is indispensable. This lesson presents those exceptions and explains why they depart from the principle.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Recall the rule that niat alone suffices for ibadat. This is the baseline.
2
Identify the exceptions
From the passage, list the four categories that require verbalization: nadhr, waqf, commencement of salah and ihram, and talaq/'itaq.
3
Analyze the rationale
For each exception, understand why verbalization is needed: nadhr and waqf involve commitment to future acts; takbir/talbiyah mark the start; talaq/'itaq affect marital status.
4
Note the common thread
Recognize that these exceptions involve matters where public manifestation or legal change is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج عن هذا الأصل مسائل : منها النذر، لا تكفي في إيجابه النية بل لا بدَّ من التلفة به، صرحوا به في باب الاعتكاف . ومنها الوقف، ولو مسجدًا لا بدَّ من التلفّظ الدَّالّ عليه. وأما توقف شروعه في الصلاة والإحرام على الذكر ولا تكفي النيَّة فلأنه من الشرائط للشروع. وأما الطلاق والعتاق فلا يقعان بالنيَّة، بل لا بد من اللفظ، إلا في مسألة فتاوی قاضي خان رجل له امرأتان عمرة وزينب فقال يا زينب، فأجابته عمرة، فقال : أنت طالق ثلاثا وقع الطلاق على التي أجابت إن كانت امرأته، وإن لم تكن امرأته بطل لأنه أخرج الجواب جوابًا لكلام التي أجابته وإن قال نويت زينب طلقت زينب .. اهـ ) . فقد وقع الطلاق على زينب بمجرد النية
Cases are excepted from this principle: Among them is nadhr (vow): intention is not sufficient to make it binding; rather verbalization is necessary, as they stated in the chapter of i'tikaf. Among them is waqf: even if it is a mosque, verbalization indicating it is necessary. As for the commencement of salah and ihram being conditional upon utterance (takbir/talbiyah) and intention not being sufficient, that is because it is a condition for commencement. As for talaq and 'itaq: they do not occur by mere intention; rather verbalization is necessary, except in the case from Fatawa Qadikhan: a man had two wives, 'Amrah and Zaynab; he said 'O Zaynab,' and 'Amrah answered; then he said 'You are divorced thrice' – the divorce fell on the one who answered if she was his wife, and if she was not his wife it is invalid because he made his answer a response to the one who answered him; if he said 'I intended Zaynab,' Zaynab is divorced. So divorce fell on Zaynab by mere intention.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage lists exceptions to the second principle that intention alone is sufficient. The exceptions require verbalization because they involve legal effects that depend on manifest expression: (1) Nadhr (vows): mere intention does not make the vow binding; one must utter it explicitly, as explained in i'tikaf. (2) Waqf: even for a mosque, verbalization is needed. (3) For starting salah and ihram, the takbir and talbiyah are required as commencement conditions; intention alone is insufficient because these acts involve transition. (4) Talaq and 'itaq do not occur by intention alone; verbalization is required. An exception is cited from Fatawa Qadikhan: in an ambiguous scenario where a man uses a name, the wife who answers may be divorced, and if he clarifies his intention later, the intended wife is divorced based on his niat. The exception is limited to such ambiguous situations.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship; requires verbalization to become binding.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment of property for charitable purposes; verbalization is required to establish it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is verbalization required for talaq and 'itaq, according to the exceptions listed?
A) Because they are acts of worship
B) Because they involve legal effects that depend on manifest expression
C) Because intention is never considered in these matters
D) Because the second principle is invalid

Key Takeaway
Verbalization is required for vows, endowments, commencement of salah and ihram, and marriage-related acts, even though intention alone suffices for most acts of worship.

When Intention Clarifies Ambiguous Verbalization: The Case from Fatawa Qadikhan
Exception to the exception in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the istithna' (exception) to the exception in talaq, where intention can determine the outcome of an ambiguous utterance.

Core Idea
When a divorce utterance is ambiguous, the man's intention after the fact decides which wife is divorced.

Though talaq generally requires verbalization, there is a specific scenario from the Hanafi mufti literature where intention plays a decisive role. In this lesson, we analyze the case from Fatawa Qadikhan: a man with two wives addresses one name, the other answers, and he says 'you are divorced thrice.' The outcome hinges on his intention.

Guided Path
1
Set the scenario
A man has two wives 'Amrah and Zaynab. He calls 'O Zaynab,' but 'Amrah answers. He then says 'You are divorced thrice.'
2
Identify the ambiguity
Who is 'you' referring to? The one who answered or the one named?
3
Apply the default rule
Verbalization alone: if he intended the one who answered, she is divorced; if she is not his wife, the talaq is invalid. But if he says 'I intended Zaynab,' then Zaynab is divorced.
4
Understand the exception
In this ambiguous utterance, the niat (intention) after the fact determines the outcome. This is an exception to the rule that niat alone is insufficient for talaq.

Source Text

Cases are excepted from this principle: Among them is nadhr (vow): intention is not sufficient to make it binding; rather verbalization is necessary, as they stated in the chapter of i'tikaf. This passage lists exceptions to the second principle that intention alone is sufficient. (2) Waqf: even for a mosque, verbalization is needed. الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment of property for charitable purposes; verbalization is required to establish it.

The Five Stages of Inner Thoughts and Their Rulings
Defining the five stages of internal monologue and their legal accountability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five stages of inner thoughts (khawatir) and apply the Hanafi ruling for each, including the dispute over azm (firm resolve).

Core Idea
The first three inner thoughts are forgiven; firm resolve (azm) to sin is punishable.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazai'r elaborates on the prophetic hadith that Allah does not hold people accountable for what they think as long as they do not speak or act. The Hanafi jurists have classified inner thoughts into five distinct stages with varying legal consequences. Understanding these stages is crucial for muftis to differentiate between mere passing thoughts and punishable resolve.

Guided Path
1
List the five stages in order
Identify the five terms: hajis, khatir, hadith al-nafs, hamm, azm. Understand their sequence from initial fleeting thought to firm resolution.
The order is cumulative; each stage builds on the previous.
2
Assign the legal ruling to each stage
For each stage, determine whether it is accountable (mu'akhadh), lifted (marbu'), or recorded. Use the hadith as the core principle.
Hajis: not accountable by consensus. Khatir and hadith al-nafs: lifted. Hamm: good deeds recorded, evil deeds not. Azm: punishable.
3
Note the dispute over azm
Some scholars consider azm as part of the lifted hamm, but the majority (muhaqqiqun) hold it punishable. Understand the evidence on both sides.
The opposing view relies on generalizing the hadith; the majority distinguishes azm as a decisive resolve.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
For fatwa, the relied-upon position is that azm is punishable (except for acts completed by resolve alone, like apostasy). Learn the practical consequence.
The passage cites al-Bazzaziyyah to reinforce this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها حديث النفس لا يؤاخذ به ما لم يتكلم أو يعمل به كما في حديث مسلم. وحاصل ما قالوه؛ إن الذي يقع في النفس من قصد المعصية أو الطاعة على خمس مراتب الهاجس وهو ما يلقى فيها، ثم جريانه فيها وهو الخاطر، ثم حديث النفس، وهو ما يقع فيها من التردّد؛ هل يفعل أو لا، ثم الهم وهو ترجيح قصد الفعل، ثم العزم وهو قوَّة ذلك القصد والجزم به. فالهاجس لا يؤاخذ به إجماعا لأنه ليس من فعله وإنما هو شيء ورد عليه لا قدرة له ولا صنع. والخاطر الذي بعده كان قادرًا على دفعه بصرف الهاجس أول وروده، ولكنه هو وما بعده من حديث النفس مرفوعان بالحديث الصحيح. وإذا ارتفع حديث النفس ارتفع ما قبله بالأولى. وهذه الثلاثة لو كانت في الحسنات لم يُكتب له بها أخر لعدم القصد . وأما الهم فقد بيّن في الحديث الصحيح أن الهم بالحسنة يُكتب حسنة والهم بالسيئة لا يُكتب سيئة، وينتظر فإن تركها الله تعالى كتبت حسنة وإن فعلها كتبت سيئة واحدة. والأصح في معناه أن يكتب عليه الفعل وحده وهو معنى قوله واحدة، وإن الهم مرفوع . وأما العزم فالمحققون على أنه يؤاخذ به، ومنهم من جعله من الهم المرفوع . وفي البزازية من كتاب الكراهية هَمَّ بمعصية لا يأثم إن لم يصمم عزمه عليها، وإن عزم أثِمَ إثم العزم لا إثم العمل بالجوارح، إلا أن يكون أمرًا يتم بمجرد العزم كالكفر . (اهـ).
And among them is the hadith of inner speech: one is not held accountable for it unless he speaks or acts upon it, as in the hadith of Muslim. In summary, what they have said: that what occurs in the mind of intent for sin or obedience is of five levels: al-hajis, which is what is cast into it; then its pursuing in it, which is al-khatir; then hadith al-nafs, which is what occurs in it of hesitation whether to do or not; then al-hamm, which is the preponderance of the intention to act; then al-azm, which is the strength of that intention and resolution. Al-hajis is not held against him by consensus, because it is not his act but something that comes upon him without his power or doing. Al-khatir which follows: he was able to repel it by turning away from the hajis at its first occurrence, but it and what follows from hadith al-nafs are both lifted by the authentic hadith. And if hadith al-nafs is lifted, what comes before it is lifted even more so. These three, if they are in good deeds, no reward is written for him due to lack of intent. As for al-hamm, it is clarified in the authentic hadith that the intention of a good deed is written as a good deed, and the intention of an evil deed is not written as an evil deed; rather it is waited: if he abandons it for Allah, a good deed is written, and if he does it, one evil deed is written. The more correct interpretation is that the act alone is written, which is the meaning of 'one', and that the hamm is lifted. As for al-azm, the verifiers hold that one is held accountable for it, and among them are those who considered it from the lifted hamm. In al-Bazzaziyyah from Kitab al-Karahiyyah: one who intends a sin does not sin unless he resolves firmly upon it; if he resolves, he sins the sin of resolution, not the sin of action with the limbs, unless it is something that is accomplished by mere resolution, like disbelief.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the famous hadith that Allah does not hold people accountable for what they think as long as they do not speak or act. It defines five levels: hajis (initial thought), khatir (passing thought), hadith al-nafs (self-talk), hamm (preponderant intention), and azm (firm resolution). The first three are not recorded at all, whether for good or evil. Hamm: for a good deed it is recorded as a good deed; for an evil deed, if one abstains for Allah, a good deed is recorded, but if acted upon, one sin. Azm: the majority hold that firm resolution is blameworthy, and it incurs sin for the resolve itself, not the action, except for acts that are completed by resolve alone, like apostasy.

Key Terms
الهاجس (al-hajis): The first fleeting thought that crosses the mind without conscious effort
الخاطر (al-khatir): A thought that follows the hajis and one can repel it
حديث النفس (hadith al-nafs): Internal deliberation or hesitation about doing an act
الهم (al-hamm): A preponderant intention to act, short of firm resolution
العزم (al-azm): Firm resolve and determination to commit the act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which stage of inner thought is considered punishable?
A) Al-hajis
B) Al-khatir
C) Al-hamm
D) Al-azm

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the first three stages of inner thoughts are forgiven, while firm resolve (azm) to sin incurs sin, except for acts completed by resolve alone.

Necessity of Verbal Utterance for Waqf, Salah, Talaq, and 'Itq
Explaining which acts require verbal utterance and where intention suffices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts require verbal utterance and apply the exception where intention suffices for talaq according to Fatawa Qadhi Khan.

Core Idea
Intention alone is insufficient for waqf, salah, talaq, and 'itaq; utterance is required except in a specific talaq case.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, certain acts demand explicit verbal articulation (talfazz) to be legally effective. This passage clarifies the boundaries of intention (niyyah) versus utterance, covering waqf, the commencement of salah, divorce, and manumission. It also presents a notable exception from Fatawa Qadhi Khan where intention alone determines the effect of a divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts requiring utterance
List the four acts mentioned: waqf (endowment), the start of salah and ihram, talaq (divorce), and 'itaq (manumission). Note the general rule: intention alone is not enough; verbal expression is necessary.
The passage states 'la budda min al-talfazz' (utterance is necessary) for these.
2
Understand the rationale for salah and ihram
For salah and ihram, the requirement of verbal remembrance (dhikr) is justified as a condition for commencement (shurut li al-shuru'). Intention alone does not suffice because the act begins with a verbal declaration.
This connects to the Hanafi principle that certain acts have a formal starting point.
3
Analyze the exception in talaq
The passage cites Fatawa Qadhi Khan: if a man calls one wife but another replies, and he pronounces divorce, intention determines the addressee. If he intended the other wife, the divorce is effective on her by mere intention.
This is a narrow exception; note the footnote questioning its basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الوقف، ولو مسجدًا لا بدَّ من التلفّظ الدَّالّ عليه. وأما توقف شروعه في الصلاة والإحرام على الذكر ولا تكفي النيَّة فلأنه من الشرائط للشروع. وأما الطلاق والعتاق فلا يقعان بالنيَّة، بل لا بد من اللفظ، إلا في مسألة فتاوی قاضي خان رجل له امرأتان عمرة وزينب فقال يا زينب، فأجابته عمرة، فقال : أنت طالق ثلاثا وقع الطلاق على التي أجابت إن كانت امرأته، وإن لم تكن امرأته بطل لأنه أخرج الجواب جوابًا لكلام التي أجابته وإن قال نويت زينب طلقت زينب .. اهـ ) . فقد وقع الطلاق على زينب بمجرد النية (1) قال الشيخ محمد علي الرافعي الطرابلسي في تقييداته ص ٢٥ لا وجه لهذا الاستثناء، إلا معنى قوله لا تعتبر نيّة الكافر أي فيما كان عبادة ،وضعا والسفر ليس بعبادة وضعا .
And among them is waqf, even if a mosque, verbal expression indicating it is necessary. As for the postponement of its commencement in prayer and ihram upon remembrance, and intention is not sufficient, because it is from the conditions of commencement. As for divorce and manumission, they do not occur by intention; rather verbal utterance is necessary, except in a case from Fatawa Qadhi Khan: a man had two wives, Amrah and Zainab. He said, 'O Zainab,' and Amrah answered him, so he said, 'You are thrice divorced.' The divorce falls on the one who answered if she is his wife; if she is not his wife, it is invalid, because he made the answer a response to the speech of the one who answered him. If he said, 'I intended Zainab,' Zainab is divorced. So divorce occurred upon Zainab by mere intention. (1) Shaykh Muhammad Ali al-Rafi'i al-Tarabulusi said in his Taqyidat, p. 25: 'There is no basis for this exception, except the meaning of his saying: the intention of a disbeliever is not considered, meaning in what is an act of worship by nature, and travel is not an act of worship by nature.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage discusses cases where intention (niyyah) alone is not sufficient for legal efficacy. For waqf, even a mosque, verbal expression (talfazz) is necessary. For beginning salah and ihram, verbal remembrance (dhikr) is required because it is a condition for commencement. Divorce (talaq) and manumission ('itaq) do not occur by mere intention; utterance is mandatory. However, an exception is cited from Fatawa Qadhi Khan: if a man has two wives, 'Amrah and Zainab, and calls out 'O Zainab' but 'Amrah answers, and he says 'You are thrice divorced,' the divorce falls on the one who answered if she is his wife; if not, it is invalid. But if he intended Zainab, Zainab is divorced by mere intention. This shows that in such a case, intention suffices. The footnote by Shaykh Muhammad Ali al-Rafi'i explains that this exception is only valid where the act is not purely devotional.

Key Terms
التلفظ (al-talfazz): Verbal articulation of the words required for the act
النية (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart, which is sufficient for many acts but not all
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce, which requires utterance except in the exceptional case

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following requires verbal utterance?
A) Making a vow (nadhr)
B) Endowing a mosque (waqf)
C) Intending to pray
D) Fasting

Key Takeaway
Intention is not a substitute for verbal utterance in waqf, salah commencement, talaq, and 'itaq; but in a specific talaq case, intention can determine the subject of divorce.

Valid Purity of a Non-Muslim Before Conversion and Its Furu'
Ruling on non-Muslim's purity validity with a furu' example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that a non-Muslim's acts of purity are valid, and derive furu' rulings such as the Kitabiyya's ghusl not being required for marital relations.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim's wudu and ghusl are valid; upon conversion, prayer with that purification is accepted.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir establishes a key principle regarding the validity of a non-Muslim's acts of worship for worldly purposes. This principle then governs a sub-ruling about a Kitabiyya woman's menstrual purity, demonstrating how a single qawida can generate multiple furu' cases. Understanding this maxim will help you navigate similar scenarios concerning the worship of non-Muslims before conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the first part: 'So his ablution and his bath are valid, and if he embraces Islam after them, he prays with them.' This establishes that a non-Muslim's wudu and ghusl are considered valid acts of purification.
The ruling does not require intention (niyyah) for validity here.
2
Note the conditional effect
The validity becomes spiritually beneficial after conversion. The prayer performed with that purification is accepted. This shows that the acts are valid in themselves, though they only count as worship after accepting Islam.
This is a Hanafi position; other schools may differ.
3
Extract the underlying principle (qawida)
From this ruling, we deduce the maxim: 'A non-Muslim's acts of purity are valid' (طهارة الكافر صحيحة). This is the basis for the subsequent furu'.
The maxim applies to both wudu and ghusl.
4
Apply the principle to the furu' case
The text then presents a furu': For a Kitabiyya woman whose menstrual blood stops before ten days, intercourse is permitted without requiring ghusl. The reason given: 'she is not among its people' – i.e., she is not obligated to perform ghusl as a non-Muslim. However, the purity state is deemed correct because the non-Muslim's purification is valid.
This furu' shows that the principle extends to permissibility of marital relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيصح وضوءه وغسله فإذا أسلم بعدهما صلّى بهما، لكن قالوا إذا انقطع دم الكتابية لأقل من عشرة حلّ وطؤها بمجرد الانقطاع ولا يتوقف على الغسل لأنها ليست من أهله، وإن صح ما بها لصحة طهارة الكافر قبل إسلامه .
So his ablution and his bath are valid, and if he embraces Islam after them, he prays with them. But they said: If the blood of the Kitabiyya stops before ten days, intercourse with her is permitted merely by cessation and does not depend on ghusl because she is not among its people, though what she has is correct due to the validity of the purity of a non-Muslim before his Islam.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding the acts of worship of a non-Muslim before conversion. The author states that if a non-Muslim performs wudu (ablution) or ghusl (full bath) and then embraces Islam, his prayer performed with that purification is valid. The reasoning is that these acts of purification are valid per se, so when combined with the subsequent conversion, the prayer is acceptable. A sub-ruling follows concerning a Jewish or Christian woman (Kitabiyya) whose menstrual bleeding stops before the minimum duration of menstruation (ten days per Hanafi view). In this case, it is permissible for her husband to have intercourse immediately without requiring her to perform ghusl, because as a non-Muslim she is not obligated to perform the formal bath for purification. However, the text implies that the purity state is deemed correct, so no obstacle exists. The ruling is based on the principle that a non-Muslim's acts of worship are valid for worldly purposes (like purity) even if they are not spiritually rewarded.

Key Terms
وضوء (wudu): Ablution performed before prayer, considered valid even from a non-Muslim before conversion.
غسل (ghusl): Full ritual bath, valid from a non-Muslim before conversion.
كتابية (Kitabiyya): A Jewish or Christian woman, whose menstrual rulings are considered.
طهارة الكافر صحيحة (taharat al-kafir sahiha): The principle that a non-Muslim's acts of purification are valid for worldly purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a Kitabiyya's ghusl not required for intercourse after her menses stops?
A) Because she is not obligated to perform ghusl as a non-Muslim.
B) Because her menses is not considered ritually impure.
C) Because the minimum menses duration is ten days, so she is not considered impure.
D) Because she is from the People of the Book, who are exempt from ghusl.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a non-Muslim's purification acts, enabling ease in both worship and marital rulings after conversion.

Third Condition for Validity of Worship: Knowledge of the Intended Act (al-ilm bil-manwi)
Defining a condition and its exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of 'ilm bil-manwi' to distinguish valid from invalid intentions in acts of worship, and identify the exception for Hajj.

Core Idea
Without knowledge of an act's obligation, intention fails — except in Hajj where ambiguous ihram is permitted.

The validity of any act of worship depends on a sound intention (niyyah). But intention itself requires knowledge: you must know at least that the act is obligatory. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir lays down that general rule and then highlights a notable exception in Hajj, where ambiguous intention is initially accepted. Understanding this condition helps a mufti evaluate intention-related issues across different worships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Recognize that 'ilm bil-manwi (knowledge of the intended act) is stated as the third condition for validity of worship. The term 'manwi' refers to the act one intends to perform.
This is a shart (condition), not a rukn.
2
Apply the general ruling
The text gives an example: someone ignorant of the obligatory nature (fardiyya) of prayer – their prayer is invalid because the intention lacks the required knowledge.
The source references al-Qunyah for this ruling.
3
Note the exception for Hajj
Unlike prayer, the scholars validated ambiguous ihram (entering the state without specifying Hajj or Umrah). This is based on the action of Ali (ra) who entered ihram for what the Prophet entered ihram.
The exception shows that knowledge of the specific type (Hajj vs Umrah) is not required at the outset in Hajj.
4
Parse the limitation of the exception
If the pilgrim specifies Hajj or Umrah before commencing the actions (tawaf, sa'i), it is valid. But if the pilgrim starts the actions without specification, the intention becomes determined as Umrah.
This prevents indefinite ambiguity once actions begin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : العلم بالمنوي فمن جهل فرضية الصلاة لم تصح كما قدمناه القنية، إلا في الحج فإنهم صححوا الإحرام المبهم لأن عليا أحرم بما أحرم النبي وصححه فإن عين حجا أو عمرة صح إن كان قبل الشروع في الأفعال، وإن شرع تعينت عمرة .
The third: knowledge of the intended (act). So whoever is ignorant of the obligatoriness of prayer, his prayer is not valid, as we presented in al-Qunyah. Except in Hajj, for they validated ambiguous ihram because Ali entered ihram for what the Prophet entered ihram and it was validated. So if he specifies Hajj or Umrah, it is valid if it is before commencing the actions, but if he begins, it becomes determined as Umrah.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage states the third condition for the validity of worship: the person must have knowledge ('ilm) of what he intends. Specifically, if a person is ignorant of the obligation (fardiyya) of prayer, his prayer is not valid because he cannot form a proper intention to fulfill an obligation he does not know. The author references al-Qunyah for this ruling. There is an exception for Hajj: the scholars validated an ambiguous ihram (say, intending the pilgrimage without specifying Hajj or Umrah). This is based on the precedent of Ali (ra) who entered ihram for what the Prophet (pbuh) entered ihram, and this was considered valid. If the pilgrim later specifies Hajj or Umrah before commencing the actions (tawaf, sa'i), it is valid; but if he starts the actions, the intention becomes Umrah (determined). This exception shows that knowledge of the exact type is not required for hajj at the outset, unlike prayer.

Key Terms
العلم بالمنوي (al-ilm bil-manwi): Knowledge of the intended act; requires knowing it is an obligation.
إحرام مبهم (ihram mubham): Ambiguous ihram: entering ihram without specifying Hajj or Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if one begins the actions of Hajj after an ambiguous intention without specification?
A) The intention becomes Hajj.
B) The intention becomes Umrah.
C) The Hajj is invalid.
D) It depends on the type of action started.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an act's obligation is essential for valid intention; however, Hajj uniquely permits ambiguous intention at the outset, following the precedent of Ali.

Takhsis al-'Amm bi al-Niyyah in Oaths: Diyanatan vs. Qada
Qawida with furuat and istithna' on intention in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'takhsis al-amm bi al-niyyah' to distinguish diyanatan from qadan rulings in oath cases, identify the exception for coercion, and construct a fatwa using the Hanafi hierarchy.

Core Idea
Specifying a general oath by intention is accepted religiously, not legally — coercion is an exception.

When someone makes a general oath, can they later claim a specific intention to limit its scope? This passage lays down a key legal maxim (qa'ida) in Hanafi law: such specification is accepted in conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qadan), with an important exception for those under coercion. The dispute with Imam al-Khassaf and the practical relief for the oppressed will help you navigate both the strict rule and its merciful flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida
Extract the main legal maxim: 'تخصيص العام بالنية مقبولة ديانة لا قضاء'. Note the two dimensions: religious conscience (diyanatan) and legal enforcement (qadan).
2
Analyze the Dispute (Khassaf)
Understand that Imam al-Khassaf holds it is accepted even legally (qadan). The dominant madhhab (zahir al-madhhab) rejects this, but his view is a valid relief under coercion.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Apply the Furuat
Work through the examples: divorce (‘every woman I marry is divorced’ — not accepted legally), manumission (‘every slave I own is free’ — diyanatan accepted if claiming males, but not if claiming blacks). Why the difference?
The distinction hinges on plausibility: claiming a subset that is natural (males) vs. arbitrary (color).
4
Identify the Istithna' (Exception)
Note the exception for coercion (ظالم). In such cases, the mufti may permit following Khassaf's view to relieve hardship, based on the qawida 'الضرر يزال'.
This shows the fatwa hierarchy: the dominant view is primary, but necessity allows departure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة في الأيمان: تخصيص العام بالنيَّة مقبولة ديانة لا قضاء، وعند الخصاف يصح قضاء أيضًا؛ فلو قال: كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثم قال: نويت من بلدة كذا. لم يصح في ظاهر المذهب خلافًا للخصاف. وكذا من غصب دراهم إنسان. فلما حلفه الخصم عاما نوى خاصا وما قاله الخصاف مخلص لمن حلفه ظالم والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب فتى وقع في يد الظلمة، وأخذ بقول الخصاف فلا بأس به. كذا في الولوالجية. ولو قال: كل مملوك أملكه فهو حرّ. وقال عنيت به الرجال دون النساء، دين بخلاف ما لو قال: نويت السود دون البيض، أو بالعكس لم يصدق ديانة أيضًا كقوله نويت النساء دون الرجال والفرق بيناه في الشرح من باب اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق. وأما تعميم الخاص بالنية فلم أره الآن.
Rule on oaths: Specifying the general by intention is accepted in religion (diyanatan) not in judgment (qadan). According to al-Khassaf, it is valid in judgment as well. So if one says: 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' then says: 'I intended from a certain town,' it is not valid in the apparent school, contrary to al-Khassaf. Similarly, if one usurped someone's dirhams, and the claimant made him swear a general oath, and he intended a specific meaning—what al-Khassaf said is a relief for one who is sworn by a tyrant. The fatwa is on the apparent school, but if one falls into the hands of oppressors and adopts al-Khassaf's opinion, there is no harm. Thus in al-Walwaljiyya. And if one says: 'Every slave I own is free,' and says: 'I meant the males, not the females,' he is believed religiously, unlike if he said: 'I meant the black ones, not the white,' or vice versa, he is not believed even religiously, just as if he said: 'I meant the women, not the men.' And we have explained the difference in the commentary on the chapter of oaths of divorce and manumission. As for generalizing the specific by intention, I have not seen it now.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a rule (qa'ida) on oaths regarding the application of intention to specify a general statement. The principle: if someone makes a general oath, his subsequent claim of a specific intention (takhsis al-'amm bi al-niyyah) is accepted in religious conscience (diyanatan) but not in a court of law (qadan). Imam al-Khassaf differs, allowing it even legally. Examples: saying 'every woman I marry is divorced' and later claiming he intended a specific locality—this is rejected in the dominant school. For a person forced to swear by a tyrant, adopting Khassaf's view is excusable (as per al-Walwaljiyya). Further distinctions: if someone says 'every slave I own is free' and claims he meant only males, he is believed diyanatan; but if he claims a distinction based on color (black vs. white) or gender in the opposite way (women not men), he is not believed even diyanatan. The author notes the difference is explained elsewhere. Finally, the opposite case (generalizing a specific statement by intention) is not found by the author. This shows the Hanafi methodology: the dominant school (zahir al-madhhab) is strict, but practical relief (takhfif) is allowed under coercion.

Key Terms
تخصيص العام بالنية (takhsis al-'amm bi al-niyyah): Claiming that the speaker's intention limited the general scope of an oath
ديانة (diyanatan): Acceptable in the court of conscience (between the person and Allah), not enforceable legally
قضاء (qadan): Enforceable in a court of law

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dominant Hanafi school, if someone says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and later claims he intended women from a specific town, is his claim accepted in court?
A) Yes, because intention is paramount.
B) No, it is not accepted in court, but accepted diyanatan.
C) It is accepted only if he has witnesses.
D) It depends on the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts specification of a general oath by intention only in religious conscience, not in court, unless coercion allows the exceptional Khassaf view.

Whose Intention Prevails in Oaths
Rule on whose intention determines the meaning of an oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim on oaths and apply it to determine whose intention governs the interpretation of an oath based on whether the oath-taker is oppressed or an oppressor.

Core Idea
An oath is interpreted by the intention of the taker if he is oppressed, and by the intention of the administrator if the taker is an oppressor.

This lesson examines a concise but vital legal maxim (qa'ida) from al-Khulasa that governs oath interpretation in disputes. The maxim balances fairness by looking at the relative power of the parties: the oppressed party's intention controls, while an oppressor's oath is interpreted against his interest. As a mufti, you must identify the status of the oath-taker to apply the correct intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Recognize the oath taker (al-halif) and the oath administrator (al-mustahlif). The administrator is typically a judge or person demanding the oath.
2
Determine the taker's status
Assess whether the oath taker is mazlum (oppressed) or zalim (oppressor) in the context of the dispute.
This requires knowing the facts and power dynamics.
3
Apply the corresponding rule
If mazlum, interpret the oath according to the taker's intention. If zalim, interpret according to the administrator's intention.
This overrides the general principle that oaths are on the taker's intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة فيها أيضًا: اليمين على نيَّة الحالف إن كان مظلومًا، وعلى نيَّة المستحلف إن كان ظالمًا كذا في الخلاصة.
Another rule: The oath is according to the intention of the one taking the oath if he is oppressed, and according to the intention of the one administering the oath if he is an oppressor. Thus in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a concise legal maxim (qa'ida) sourced from al-Khulasa, a Hanafi work. It establishes who consideration is given to when interpreting the meaning of an oath: if the person taking the oath is an oppressed party (mazlum), his own intention is used to interpret the oath's scope. If he is an oppressor (zalim), the oath is interpreted according to the intention of the person who administered the oath (mustahlif). This rule balances the need to protect the vulnerable from tyrannical use of oaths while preventing the oppressor from exploiting technicalities. The rule is practical for muftis and judges dealing with disputes involving oaths.

Key Terms
الحالف (al-halif): The person who takes the oath
المستحلف (al-mustahlif): The person who administers or demands the oath
مظلوم (mazlum): Oppressed party, whose intention controls the oath's interpretation
ظالم (zalim): Oppressor, whose oath is interpreted by the administrator's intention

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a judge administers an oath to a wealthy oppressor in a dispute, whose intention determines the meaning of the oath?
A) The oppressor's intention.
B) The judge's intention (as administrator).
C) Both intentions are considered equally.
D) The intention of the victim.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of an oath is not solely based on the taker's intention; it shifts to the administrator if the taker is an oppressor, ensuring fairness.

Intention with Doubt: The Hanafi Rasm al-Mufti Rule
Expansion of a rasm al-mufti rule on intention with doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule on intention with doubt to cases of fasting, missed prayers, and identifying congregational prayer.

Core Idea
Unconditional doubt invalidates intention; conditional intention on description is valid if the object is specified.

Doubt about time or type of worship is a common scenario. Hanafi jurisprudence provides a nuanced rule: absolute uncertainty invalidates intention, but if the worshipper ties the intention to a conditional description (e.g., 'if it is Ramadan, then obligatory'), the act is valid. This lesson unpacks the rule and its practical applications in fasting and prayer, drawing on later fatwa authorities like Fath al-Qadir and Khizanat al-Akmal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt scenario
Determine whether the doubt is about the time or the type of worship (e.g., day of doubt: Sha'ban or Ramadan?; unknown prayer: obligatory or tarawih?).
The doubt must be genuine, not a mere whim.
2
Distinguish unconditional vs conditional intention
If the person intends without condition (e.g., 'I am fasting today' without linking to the day's actual category), the intention is invalid if doubt persists. But if the person says 'I intend to fast: if it is Sha'ban, then nafl; if Ramadan, then obligatory', this is valid because the object (fasting) is specified, and the description is left conditional.
The key is that the core act (sawm, salat) is fixed in the heart.
3
Apply to the day of doubt
Fasting on yawm al-shakk without conditional intention: if later revealed as Sha'ban, not a valid fast; if Ramadan, invalid because no certainty. But with conditional intention: valid as either obligatory or nafl depending on reality.
The text explicitly validates conditional intention: 'sahhat niyyatuhu'.
4
Extend to prayer cases
Apply the same logic: missed prayer made up with doubt about whether it was already made up – even if it becomes clear it was due, it does not suffice. Similarly, praying while doubting time entry invalidates. However, joining an unknown prayer (obligatory vs tarawih): intend the obligatory with conditional that if it is not, you will repeat it; then if it turns out to be tarawih, it counts as nafl.
This shows the rule's consistency: certainty is needed unless the description is left open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوم الشك إن كان من شعبان ليس بصائم، وإن كان من رمضان كان صائمًا لم تصح نيَّته، ولو ردَّد في الوصف بأن نوى إن كان من شعبان فنفل وإلا فعن رمضان صحت نيته كما بيناه في الصوم. وينبغي على هذا أنه لو كان عليه فائتة فشك أنه قضاها أو لا، فقضاها ثم تبيَّن أنها كانت عليه أن لا تجزيه للشك وعدم الجزم بتعيينها، ولو شك في دخول وقت العبادة فأتى بها فيان أنه فعلها في الوقت لم تجزه أخذا من قولهم كما في فتح القدير: لو صلَّى الفرض وعنده أن الوقت لم يدخل فظهر أنه دخل لا تجزيه . (اهـ). وفي خزانة الأكمل أدرك القوم في الصلاة ولا يدري أنها المكتوبة أو الترويحة يكبر وينوي المكتوبة على أنها إن لم تكن مكتوبة يقضيها؛ يعني العشاء فإذا هو في العشاء صح، وإن كان في الترويحة يقع نفلاً . (اهـ).
The day of doubt: if it is from Sha'ban, he is not fasting; if it is from Ramadan, he is fasting, his intention is not valid. And if he wavered in the description by intending that if it is from Sha'ban then supererogatory, otherwise from Ramadan, his intention is valid as we explained in fasting. Based on this, it follows that if he had a missed prayer and he doubts whether he made it up or not, so he prays it, then it becomes clear that it was due from him, that it does not suffice due to doubt and lack of certainty in specifying it. And if he doubts the entry of the time of worship and performs it, then it becomes clear that he did it in time, it does not suffice, taken from their statement as in Fath al-Qadir: If he prayed the obligatory prayer while thinking that the time had not entered, then it appears that it had entered, it does not suffice. And in Khizanat al-Akmal: A group caught up in prayer and he does not know whether it is the obligatory or the tarawih, he says takbir and intends the obligatory, with the condition that if it was not obligatory he would make it up, meaning the Isha, so if it is Isha it is valid, and if it was tarawih it falls as supererogatory.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling about the necessity of certainty in intention. The author begins with the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) — fasting on a day uncertain to be from Sha'ban or Ramadan. If it later becomes clear that it was from Sha'ban, he is not considered a faster at all. If it was from Ramadan, his intention was not valid because he lacked certainty. However, if he made his intention conditional on the description — intending an optional fast if it is Sha'ban and the obligatory Ramadan fast if it is Ramadan — then the intention is valid. This is because the object (the fast) is specified in his heart, even though the description is uncertain. This principle extends to missed prayers: if one has a missed prayer and is uncertain whether he made it up, then performs it, it does not suffice if it later becomes clear that it was still due, due to doubt. Similarly, praying while doubting the entry of time is invalid even if time actually entered, because intention requires certainty. An exception from Khizanat al-Akmal: if a person joins a congregational prayer unsure whether it is the obligatory prayer or tarawih, he should intend the obligatory; if it turns out to be tarawih, it counts as nafl, because he was prepared to repeat the obligatory.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt about the time or nature of an act of worship
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The 30th of Sha'ban when the sighting of the Ramadan moon is uncertain
التّرديد في الوصف (al-tardid fi al-wasf): Leaving the description (obligatory/nafl) conditional on the reality

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who performs a missed prayer while doubting whether he already made it up, but it later becomes clear the prayer was still due?
A) The prayer suffices because he performed it.
B) The prayer does not suffice because of doubt.
C) The prayer is valid only if he intended it as qada.
D) The prayer is valid because intention is not required for qada.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, intention with doubt is invalid unless the description is left conditional while the core act is specified.

Effect of 'In Sha Allah' on Intention-based vs. Utterance-based Acts
Branch ruling on coupling intention with mashi'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intention-based acts (niyyat) and utterance-based acts (aqwal) and apply the Hanafi rule to determine the validity of an act when coupled with 'in sha Allah'.

Core Idea
In sha Allah nullifies verbal acts but not intention-based acts.

This branch (far') from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir illustrates a key Hanafi distinction: acts rooted in internal resolve (niyyat) are untouched by spoken exceptions, while those requiring definitive utterance become invalid when doubt is introduced. A mufti must quickly identify which category a given case falls into.

Guided Path
1
Classify the act as intention-based or utterance-based
Determine whether the act's legal existence relies primarily on internal intention (e.g., fasting, prayer) or on a verbal formula (e.g., divorce, manumission).
Intention-based acts are acts of worship where niyyah is a condition; utterance-based are acts where a specific spoken formula is the cause of the legal effect.
2
Apply the rule to the classified act
If intention-based, saying 'in sha Allah' does not invalidate the act; if utterance-based, it does.
The rationale: intention is internal and unaffected by spoken doubt; utterances require certainty and finality.
3
Identify the thamarah (practical consequence)
For example, a divorce pronounced with 'in sha Allah' does not count as a divorce in Hanafi law, while an intention to fast remains valid.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school generally does not invalidate divorce with 'in sha Allah' unless the speaker intended to suspend the effect. This difference has practical implications for issuing fatwa.
This cross-madhhab comparison helps a mufti understand when to follow the Hanafi rule and when to consider another view per the maslak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع: عقب النيَّة بالمشيئة ؛ قدَّمنا أنه إن كان مما يتعلق بالنيَّات كالصوم والصلاة لم تبطل وإن كان مما يتعلق بالأقوال كالطلاق والعتاق بطل.
Branch: Coupling the intention with 'if Allah wills' (mashi'a). We have previously stated that if it is among matters related to intentions, like fasting and prayer, it does not become invalid; and if it is among matters related to statements, like divorce and manumission, it becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This brief branch (far') distinguishes between acts that are essentially based on intention (niyyat) and those that are based on verbal utterance (aqwal). If one says, 'I intend to fast, in sha Allah,' or 'I intend to pray, in sha Allah,' the intention remains valid because the ruling of these acts relies on the internal resolve, and the exception does not strip the intention of its effect. However, if one says in a verbal pronouncement, such as 'You are divorced, in sha Allah,' or 'You are free, in sha Allah,' the pronouncement is nullified because the exception introduces doubt in the verbal formula, which requires definitiveness in the Hanafi madhhab. This is a well-established rule in the chapter of oaths and divorce.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'a): The phrase 'if Allah wills' used as an exception

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman says, 'I intend to pray Fajr, in sha Allah.' Later she does not pray. Is her intention valid?
A) Yes, intention is valid.
B) No, intention is invalid.
C) Intention is valid but prayer would be invalid.
D) Depends on her tone.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'in sha Allah' nullifies utterance-based acts but not intention-based acts – a crucial maxim for fatwa in divorce and worship.

Specifying General Oaths by Intention: Diyanatan vs Qada'an
Qawida with furuat on intention in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'takhsis al-amm bi al-niyyah maqbula diyanatan la qada'an' to new cases and identify when the exception of al-Khassaf applies.

Core Idea
Intent to limit a general oath is accepted between you and Allah, but not before the qadi.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between what is valid in the religious conscience (diyanatan) and what is enforceable in court (qada'an). This qawida from the chapter of oaths establishes a key rule: if someone swears a general oath and later claims he intended only a specific subset, that specification is accepted religiously but not judicially—unless the situation involves oppression, where al-Khassaf's ruling provides a lawful exit.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its scope
Read the Arabic qawida: تخصيص العام بالنيَّة مقبولة ديانة لا قضاء. It applies to oaths, divorce, and manumission.
2
Understand the diyanatan/qada'an distinction
Diyanatan: between the person and Allah, accepted in conscience. Qada'an: in court, enforceable by a judge. The qawida says specification by intention is accepted diyanatan only.
3
Apply to the first furu': marriage divorce oath
A man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced', then claims he meant only women from a certain town. Under the apparent madhhab, this is not accepted in court (qada'an) but is accepted diyanatan.
Al-Khassaf differs: he would accept it even in court as an exit for the oppressed.
4
Apply to the second furu': slave emancipation oath
A man says 'Every slave I own is free', then claims he meant only males. This is accepted diyanatan because gender is a legally relevant category. But if he claims he meant only black or white slaves, it is not accepted even diyanatan because color is not a legally distinguishing category.
The text explicitly excludes color-based distinction.
5
Note the istithna' and its limits
Al-Khassaf's view (accepted qada'an) is a lawful exit for those oppressed. However, the fatwa in the madhhab is on the apparent school. A mufti may advise using al-Khassaf's view only in genuine oppression.
The text says 'والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب' – the fatwa is on the apparent school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة في الأيمان : تخصيص العام بالنيَّة مقبولة ديانة لا قضاء، وعند الخصاف يصح قضاء أيضًا؛ فلو قال : كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثم قال : نويت من بلدة كذا. لم يصح في ظاهر المذهب خلافًا للخصاف. وكذا من غصب دراهم إنسان. فلما حلفه الخصم عاما نوى خاصا وما قاله الخصاف مخلص لمن حلفه ظالم والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب فتى وقع في يد الظلمة، وأخذ بقول الخصاف فلا بأس به. كذا في الولوالجية. ولو قال : كل مملوك أملكه فهو حرّ. وقال عنيت به الرجال دون النساء، دين بخلاف ما لو قال : نويت السود دون البيض، أو بالعكس لم يصدق ديانة أيضًا كقوله نويت النساء دون الرجال والفرق بيناه في الشرح من باب اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق. وأما تعميم الخاص بالنية فلم أره الآن.
A base concerning oaths: Specifying a general oath by intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qada'an). According to al-Khassaf, it is valid judicially as well. For example, if someone says, 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' then says, 'I intended (the women) of a specific town,' it is not valid in the apparent school, contrary to al-Khassaf. Likewise, someone who usurped another's dirhams, when the opponent made him swear an oath generally, he intended specifically. What al-Khassaf said is a way out for one who is made to swear by an oppressor. The fatwa is on the apparent school. However, if a youth falls into the hands of oppressors and adopts the opinion of al-Khassaf, there is no harm. This is in al-Walwalijiyya. And if someone says, 'Every slave I own is free,' and says, 'I meant males, not females,' it is believed religiously. Contrary to if he said, 'I intended black (slaves) not white,' or vice versa, he is not believed even religiously, like his saying, 'I intended women, not men.' The difference we explained in the commentary in the chapter of oaths of divorce and manumission. As for generalizing the specific by intention, I have not come across it now.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This qawida (base) from the chapter of oaths establishes a key rule: if someone makes a general oath but later claims he intended it only for a specific subset, his claim is accepted in his religious conscience (diyanatan) but not in a court of law (qada'an). The exception is the view of al-Khassaf, who allows it judicially. The author gives examples: a man swears that every woman he marries is divorced, then says he meant only from a certain town — this is not accepted in the dominant (zahir) madhhab. Similarly, if a usurper swears a general oath but had a specific intention, al-Khassaf's view provides a lawful exit for the oppressed. The fatwa in the madhhab is on the apparent ruling, but for someone forced by oppressors, acting on al-Khassaf's opinion is permissible. Another example: if someone says every slave he owns is free, then claims he meant only males, he is believed diyanatan because males are a distinct subset from females. But if he claims he meant only blacks or only whites, he is not believed even diyanatan, because color is not a legally distinguishing category for slaves in terms of ownership and emancipation. The author notes he has not seen the opposite (generalizing a specific oath by intention) discussed.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyanatan): Between the person and Allah; accepted in religious conscience
قضاء (qada'an): Judicially; enforceable in court
تخصيص العام بالنية (takhsis al-amm bi al-niyyah): Specifying a general statement by a subsequent intention

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent Hanafi madhhab, if a man swears 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and later claims he only meant women from a specific city, is his claim accepted in court?
A) Yes, because of general rule of intention.
B) No, it is only accepted diyanatan.
C) Yes, according to the view of al-Khassaf.
D) No, not even diyanatan.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, specifying a general oath by intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), unless one is oppressed, in which case al-Khassaf's view may be followed as a lawful exit.

10 min
Oaths Are Built on Words, Not Objectives
Qawida with furuat on oaths and verbal utterances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-ayman mabniyya 'ala al-alfaz la 'ala al-aghrad' to determine whether an oath is broken, and distinguish between literal wording and underlying intent in related verbal utterances.

Core Idea
An oath is judged by its literal wording, not by the swearer's hidden objective.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim governing oaths, divorce, and other verbal acts. The literal wording of an oath determines its legal effect, even if the swearer's actual intent or objective is different. The passage provides several illustrative furuat and branches into related areas like zihar, divorce, and recitation, all tested against this principle.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its scope
Identify the Arabic maxim and understand that it applies to oaths, divorce, manumission, and similar verbal utterances. The key is that literal expression overrides any unspoken objective.
The qawida is 'al-ayman mabniyya 'ala al-alfaz la 'ala al-aghrad'.
2
Analyze the core furuat on oaths
Work through each example: swearing not to buy for a fils but buying for 100 dirhams (no breach); swearing not to sell for ten but selling for eleven or nine (no breach); swearing not to buy for ten but buying for eleven (breach). Understand why: the literal price term is specific.
The underlying objective (e.g., anger or desire to avoid transaction) is irrelevant.
3
Apply the qawida to divorce and manumission branches
Consider the case of calling a wife named Taliq: if he intends divorce, it occurs; if he intends just calling, no. Similarly, repeating divorce words depends on intent for restart or emphasis. The literal wording is interpreted with the speaker's expressed intent.
Here intent is considered because the word is ambiguous; the qawida still focuses on what is verbally expressed.
4
Examine intra-madhhab differences on ambiguous phrases
Analyze the expression 'You are to me like my mother': if no intention, Abu Yusuf says it is nothing, Muhammad says it is zihar. Note the disagreement and the basis: whether the literal comparison implies zihar or not.
This shows that even within Hanafi school, the application of the qawida can vary.
5
Recognize the role of intent in other verbal acts
The passage then tests the principle on recitation by a junub, prayer, and sneezing during khutba. In these, intent matters because the wording is generic; the qawida specifically governs oaths, while other acts may consider purpose.
The qawida is a wusta (intermediate) maxim; its scope is primarily oaths, but it influences related areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة فيها أيضًا : الأيمان مبنية على الألفاظ لا على الأغراض ؛ فلو اغتاظ من إنسان فحلف أنه لا يشتري له شيئًا بفلس فاشترى له شيئًا بمائة درهم لم يحنث، ولو حلف لا بعشرة فباعه بأحد عشر أو بتسعة، لم يحنث مع أن غرضه الزيادة، لكن لا حنث بلا لفظ، ولو حلف لا يشتريه بعشرة فاشتراه بأحد عشر حنث. وتمامه في تلخيص الجامع وشرحه للفارسي . فروع: لو كان اسمها طالقًا أو حرَّة فناداها ؛ إن قصد الطلاق أو العتق وقعا أو النداء فلا، أو أطلق فالمعتمد ،عدمه، ولو كرّر لفظ الطلاق، فإن قصد الاستئناف وقع الكل، أو التأكيد فواحدة ديانة والكل قضاء ؛ وكذا إذا أطلق ولو قال : أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين؛ فإن نوى مع ثنتين فثلاث دخل بها أو لا، وإلا فإن نوى وثنتين فثلاث إن كان دخل بها وإلا فواحدة كما إذا نوى الظرف أو أطلق ولو نوي الضرب والحساب فكذلك . وكذا في الإقرار ولو قال: أنت علي مثل أمي أو كأمي رجع إلى قصده لينكشف حكمه ؛ فإن قال أردت الكرامة فهو كما قال لأن التكريم بالتشبيه فاش في الكلام، وإن قال أردت الظهار فهو ظهار، لأنه تشبيه بجميعها، وإن قال أردت الطلاق فهو طلاق بائن ؛ وإن لم تكن له نيَّة فليس بشيء عندهما. وقال محمد رحمه الله : هو ظهار. وإن عنى به التحريم لا غير فعند أبي يوسف رحمه الله إيلاء، وعند محمد رحمه الله ظهار. ولو قال : أنتِ عليَّ حرام كأمي ونوى ظهارًا أو طلاقا فهو على ما نوى، وإن لم ينو فعلى قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله إيلاء وعلى قول محمد رحمه الله ظهار. ومنها لو قرأ الجنب قرآنا ؛ فإن قصد التلاوة حرم، وإن قصد الذكر فلا ولو قرأ الفاتحة في صلاته على الجنازة إن قصد الدعاء والثناء لم يكره، وإن قصد التلاوة کره عطس الخطيب فقال الحمد لله ؛ إن قصد الخطبة ،صحت ، وإن قصد الحمد للعطس لم تصح . ذبح فعطس وقال الحمد لله ، فكذلك ذكر المصلي آية أو ذكرًا وقصد به جواب المتكلم فسدت وإلا فلا.
A base in it as well: Oaths are built on words, not on objectives. So if someone becomes angry with another and swears that he will not buy anything for him for a single fils, then buys him something for a hundred dirhams, he does not break the oath. And if he swears not (to sell it) for ten, then sells it for eleven or nine, he does not break the oath, even though his objective was increase; but no breaking of oath without the word. And if he swears not to buy it for ten, then buys it for eleven, he breaks the oath. The complete discussion is in Talkhis al-Jami' and its commentary by al-Farisi. Branches: If her name was Taliq (divorced woman) or Hurrah (free woman), and he called her by that name: if he intended divorce or manumission, they occur; if he intended just calling, no; if he did not specify, the relied-upon is that it does not occur. And if he repeated the word of divorce: if he intended starting anew, all occur; if he intended emphasis, then one occurs diyanatan, and all occur judicially; similarly if he did not specify. And if he said, 'You are divorced once in two,' then if he intended 'together with two' then three (occur) whether he had consummated or not; otherwise, if he intended 'and two' then three if he had consummated, otherwise one, as if he intended the particle or did not specify. And if he intended 'strike' and 'calculation' likewise. And similarly in admissions. And if he said, 'You are to me like my mother' or 'as my mother,' the judgment depends on his intent; if he says he meant honoring, it is as he said because honoring by comparison is common in speech; if he said he meant zihar, it is zihar because it is comparison of all of her; if he said he meant divorce, it is a ba'in divorce. If he had no intention, it is nothing according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Imam Muhammad said: it is zihar. If he meant prohibition only, then according to Abu Yusuf it is ila', according to Muhammad it is zihar. And if he said, 'You are forbidden to me like my mother' and intended zihar or divorce, it is as he intended; if he did not intend, then according to Abu Yusuf it is ila', according to Muhammad zihar. Among them: if a junub reads the Quran: if he intends recitation, it is forbidden; if he intends dhikr, not. And if he reads al-Fatiha in the funeral prayer: if he intends supplication and praise, it is not disliked; if he intends recitation, it is disliked. If the khatib sneezes and says al-hamdulillah: if he intended it as part of the khutba, it is valid; if he intended thanksgiving for the sneeze, it is not valid. If during slaughter he sneezes and says al-hamdulillah, likewise. And if a praying person recites a verse or dhikr intending it as a reply to a speaker, the prayer is invalid; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This extensive passage presents the qawida that oaths are built on literal words, not underlying objectives. The author illustrates with examples: if someone swears not to buy something for a single fils, but buys it for a hundred dirhams, he does not break the oath because the word 'fils' is specific; similarly, if he swears not to sell for ten, selling for eleven or nine does not break the oath because the wording 'ten' is not matched. But if he swears not to buy for ten and buys for eleven, the oath is broken because the act of buying occurred for a different price. The branches then cover more nuanced verbal acts: calling a wife 'Taliq' (a name) — if he intends divorce, it occurs; if he intended just calling, no. Repeating 'divorce' — if he intends starting anew, all count; if emphasis, only one diyanatan but all judicially. The tricky expression 'You are divorced once in two' — the intention behind the particle matters. Then expressions like 'You are to me like my mother' — this could be zihar, divorce, or honor depending on intent. The madhhab differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are noted. The passage then moves to rulings on recitation by a junub (intention matters), funeral prayer, sneezing during khutba, and whether a praying person's reply invalidates the prayer — all governed by the principle that intention and context determine the legal effect, but for oaths specifically, the literal wording is primary.

Key Terms
الأغراض (al-aghrad): Underlying objectives or intentions behind the oath
الحنث (al-hinth): Breaking or violating an oath
الظهار (al-zihar): A pre-modern form of divorce where the husband compares his wife to his mother
الإيلاء (al-ila'): A vow to abstain from marital relations for at least four months

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not buy any property for a thousand dirhams.' Later he buys the property for 1200 dirhams. According to the qawida, did he break his oath?
A) Yes, because his objective was to avoid buying.
B) No, because the price is different.
C) Yes, because he bought the same property.
D) No, because the oath was general.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school judges oaths by their literal wording; the swearer's unexpressed objective does not affect the ruling.
